Two Types of Possibility and Sound Consideration
Distinguishes two types of possibility and explains need for sound consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between specific and general possibility and why sound consideration is essential for valid reasoning in kalam.

Core Idea
Not all reasoning that leads to truth is sound—only intrinsic validity counts.

This lesson delves into a key methodological point in Islamic theology: the nature of possibility and the requirement for soundness in rational consideration. The author clarifies that whether possibility is taken as specific (Ashʿarite) or general (encompassing all schools) affects the scope of the definition. Moreover, unsound reasoning, even if it accidentally leads to truth, is not a valid path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first a comment on 'possibility', then on 'unsound does not entail', and finally on accidental truth.
Pay attention to the parentheses which indicate quoted phrases from the original definition.
2
Understand the two types of possibility
Identify the two interpretations of 'possibility' (imkān): specific (khāṣṣ) and general (ʿāmm). Understand how each affects the scope of the definition—specific limits it to the Ashʿarites, general includes all schools.
Specific possibility excludes necessity, while general possibility includes it.
3
Grasp why unsound reasoning fails
The author explains that unsound consideration does not entail the sought object due to defect in matter or form. If the term 'consideration' were taken universally or absolutely without qualification, all paths would be excluded or indistinct.
Soundness is a necessary condition for consideration to be a valid path.
4
Recognize the accidental nature of truth from unsound reasoning
Even if unsound reasoning coincidentally leads to truth, that is accidental (ittifāqī), not because the reasoning is a genuine means. This underscores the need for intrinsic soundness.
Coincidental success does not make an unsound method valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قوله : (اعتبر الإمكان) إن أريد الإمكان الخاص يكون التعريف مختصاً برأي الأشاعرة وإن أريد الإمكان المجامع للوجوب يشمل جميع المذاهب المذكورة فيما سبق. قوله : (لأن الفاسد إلخ) أي مادة أو صورة لا يستلزمه كما عرفت فلو لم يقيد النظر بالصحيح فإن أريد به العموم خرجت الطرق بأسرها عن التعريف إذ لا يمكن التوصل بكل نظر فيها وإن اقتصر على الإطلاق لم يكن هناك تنبيه على افتراق الصحيح والفاسد في ذلك. قوله : (لا يستلزم المطلوب) وإن كان قد يفضي إليه فذلك اتفاقي ليس من حيث إنه وسيلة إليه
Regarding his statement: 'Possibility was considered' — if specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) is intended, the definition is specific to the Ashʿarite view; but if general possibility that includes necessity is intended, it encompasses all the schools mentioned previously. His statement: 'Because the unsound etc.' — that is, in matter or form, it does not entail it, as you have understood. For if consideration were not qualified by soundness, then if universality were intended, all paths would be excluded from the definition, since it is not possible to arrive through every consideration in them; and if it were confined to absolute (unqualified) consideration, there would be no indication of the distinction between sound and unsound in that regard. His statement: 'It does not entail the sought object' — and even if it may lead to it, that is by coincidence, not by virtue of being a means to it.
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on specific parts of the earlier definition. First, the term 'possibility' (al-imkān) in the definition is analyzed. If 'specific possibility' (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) is intended—possibility that excludes necessity—then the definition is exclusive to the Ashʿarite school, which holds that the path is merely possible. If 'general possibility' (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) is intended—which includes what is necessary—then the definition covers all theological schools, including those that hold that the path may be necessary. Next, the phrase 'because the unsound does not entail the sought object' is explained: unsound consideration fails to entail the goal due to defect in matter (content) or form (structure). If consideration were not qualified as 'sound' and the term were taken universally, all paths would be excluded because not every consideration leads to the goal; if taken absolutely (unqualified), there would be no way to distinguish sound from unsound reasoning. Finally, the comment 'it does not entail the sought object' addresses a potential objection: even unsound reasoning may coincidentally lead to the truth. The author responds that such a result is accidental (ittifāqī), not because the reasoning itself is a genuine means to the goal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any reasoning that leads to truth is valid.
Correction: The author stresses that unsound reasoning, even if it accidentally yields truth, is not a valid path because it lacks intrinsic soundness.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if 'specific possibility' is intended in the definition?
A) The definition applies to all schools.
B) The definition applies only to the Ashʿarite school.
C) The definition is invalid.
D) The definition requires necessity.

Key Takeaway
The validity of reasoning depends on intrinsic soundness, not accidental success.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were convinced by an argument that sounded persuasive but later turned out to be flawed. What was missing in that reasoning? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the soundness of arguments you accept in your own life?
?
The passage distinguishes between intrinsic soundness and accidental success. Are there areas of your belief or practice where you rely on 'it worked out' as proof of correctness? What would it mean to prioritize intrinsic soundness over outcomes?
?
If a reasoning process is unsound in form or content, even a true conclusion does not vindicate it. Does this apply to how you justify your own actions or beliefs? Will you commit to checking the logical structure of your reasoning before relying on it?

Classification of the Path: Identifier and Proof
Classifies the path into ma'rif and dalil with subdivisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the path of inquiry is classified into identifier (ma'rif) and proof (dalil), and how proof is further subdivided based on certainty and direction of inference.

Core Idea
The path of inquiry is classified by the type of sought object.

This lesson builds on the previous one about apprehension. Since sought objects are either concepts or assents, the path to reach them differs. The author introduces the key terms ma'rif, dalil, amara, burhan anni, and burhan limmi, laying the groundwork for understanding how Islamic theologians reason from evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the foundation
Recall that apprehension is either conceptualization or assent. The sought object in inquiry mirrors this division.
2
Learn the two main paths
Read the passage to see how the path is named based on the sought object: ma'rif for conceptualization, dalil for assent.
Ma'rif is like a definition; dalil is like an argument.
3
Examine the subdivisions of dalil
Notice that dalil is first divided into conjectural (dhanni) and certain (qat'i). Then note the further specification: sometimes dalil is restricted to certain, and conjectural is called amara.
The example: damp cloud indicates rain (conjectural); the world indicates the Creator (certain).
4
Understand the second specification
See that dalil can also be specified as inference from effect to cause (burhan anni) or from cause to effect (burhan limmi / ta'lil).
Example: fever indicates putrefaction (effect to cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان الإدراك إما تصوراً أو تصديقاً فكذا المطلوب) الإدراكي الذي يطلب بالنظر (فإن كان) المطلوب ( تصوراً سمي طريقه ) الذي يمكن أن يتوصل بالنظر فيه إليه ( معرفاً، وإن كان ( المطلوب ( تصديقا سمي) طريقه ) دليلا (وهو) أي الدليل بالمعنى المذكور ( يشمل الظني ( الموصل إلى الظن كالغيم الرطب الموصل إلى ظن المطر ( والقطعي ) الموصل إلى الجزم، والقطع كالعالم الموصل إلى العلم بوجود الصانع، ( وقد يخص ) الدليل ) بالقطعي ويسمى الظني إمارةً وقد يخص الدليل أيضاً مع التخصيص الأول ( بما يكون ) الاستدلال فيه ) من المعلول ( كالحمى ( على العلة كتعفن الأخلاط هذا برهاناً آنياً ( ويسمى عكسه) عكسه) وهو ما يستدل فيه العلة على المعلول ويسمى (تعليلاً) وبرهاناً لمياً.
And since apprehension is either conceptualization or assent, so too the apprehensive sought object that is sought through inquiry. If the sought object is a conceptualization, its path—by which it is possible to reach it through inquiry—is called an identifier (ma'rif). And if the sought object is an assent, its path is called a proof (dalil). And (it), i.e. the proof in the mentioned sense, includes the conjectural (dhanni) which leads to conjecture, like the damp cloud that leads to the conjecture of rain, and the certain (qat'i) which leads to certainty and conviction, like the world that leads to knowledge of the existence of the Maker. And (the proof) may be specified (as) the certain, and the conjectural is called an indicator (amara). And the proof may also be specified, along with the first specification, as that in which the inference is from the effect, like fever (as evidence) for the cause, like the putrefaction of humors; this is a proof of the 'that' (burhan anni). And its opposite is called, i.e. that in which the cause is inferred upon the effect, and it is called an explanatory proof (ta'lil) and a proof of the 'why' (burhan limmi).
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage classifies the path (tariq) based on the type of sought object (matlub). Since apprehension is either conceptual (tasawwur) or assertoric (tasdiq), the sought object is likewise. If the sought object is a conceptualization, its path is called an 'identifier' (ma'rif). If it is an assertion, its path is called a 'proof' (dalil). The proof is then subdivided into two types: conjectural (dhanni) leading to opinion, like a damp cloud indicating rain; and certain (qat'i) leading to certainty, like the world indicating the existence of the Creator. Further specification: sometimes 'proof' is restricted to the certain, while the conjectural is called 'indicator' (amara). Another specification: within the certain, the proof may be from effect to cause (burhan anni), e.g., fever indicating putrefaction; or from cause to effect, called ta'lil or burhan limmi.

تعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think 'proof' (dalil) always means a certain proof, but the passage shows it includes conjectural ones unless specified otherwise.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that dalil includes both conjectural and certain. It is only when specified that dalil is restricted to certain, and conjectural is called amara.

Checkpoint
What is the path called when the sought object is a conceptualization?
A) Dalil
B) Burhan
C) Ma'rif
D) Amara

Key Takeaway
The path of inquiry is classified as ma'rif for concepts and dalil for assents, and dalil itself is subdivided by certainty and by direction of inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the evidence for Allah's existence—like the intricate order of the universe—do you treat it as a certain proof (qat'i) that demands your full conviction, or do you allow doubts to relegate it to mere conjecture (dhanni)? How does your answer affect the way you live today?
?
The passage distinguishes between having a concept of something (tasawwur) and truly assenting to it (tasdiq). Are there truths about your faith—like Allah's attributes or the reality of the Hereafter—that you only conceptualize but have not fully assented to with certainty? What would it take to move from knowing about them to believing them with your whole heart?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah. Is it supported by a burhan anni (reasoning from effect to cause) or a burhan limmi (cause to effect)? Which type of proof gives you greater certainty, and why? Reflect on whether you have ever mistaken a weak indicator (amara) for a certain proof, and what you need to do to strengthen your conviction.

Two Methods to Prove No Evidence for a Thing
Sanusi enumerates two methods to prove absence of evidence and introduces the principle of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods used to prove that there is no evidence for a thing, and the principle that everything without evidence must be denied.

Core Idea
Proving something has no evidence requires either refuting all claimed evidence or showing none exists.

In this passage, Sanusi explains two methodological approaches to demonstrate that a claim lacks any supporting evidence. These methods are crucial for theological debates where one side asserts the absence of evidence for a doctrine. The passage also introduces a foundational principle: anything without evidence must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two methods
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the two distinct ways to prove absence of evidence: transmitting and refuting affirmers' proofs, and enumerating all evidence types then denying them.
The second method is described as returning to the first with extra work.
2
Understand the relationship between the two methods
Notice that both methods ultimately rely on 'non-finding' (ʿadam al-wijdan). The second method adds the burden of enumerating all possible evidence types.
Sanusi says the first method is preferable because it avoids this extra burden.
3
Grasp the second principle: obligation to deny
The passage asserts that everything without evidence must be denied. A proof is given: if not for this obligation, necessary truths would be negated.
This principle is foundational for further arguments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية ( قالوا : لا دليل عليه فيجب نفيه أما الأول ) وهو أنه لا دليل عليه ( فيثبت تارة بنقل أدلة المثبتين لذلك الشيء ) وبيان ضعفها وفسادها مع عدم وجدان دليل سواها ( وأخرى بحصر وجوه الأدلة ثم نفيها أي نفي الوجوه كلها (بالاستقراء) أي تتبعناها فلم نجد هاهنا شيئاً منها ( وهو عائد إلى الأول ) إذ ماله إلى عدم الوجدان ( مع مزيد مؤنة ) هو بيان حصر وجوه الأدلة فالتمسك بالأول أولى لتسقط هذه المؤنة ( وأما الثاني) وهو أن كل ما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه فيثبتونه بوجهين أشار إلى الأول بقوله : ( فإذ (لولاه أي لولا وجوب نفي ما لا دليل عليه ( انتفت الضروريات
The sixth observation - the fifth objective: What are the strong methods? (They said: There is no evidence for it, so its negation is obligatory. As for the first), i.e. that there is no evidence for it, (it is established sometimes by transmitting the evidences of those who affirm that thing) and showing their weakness and corruption, together with not finding any evidence besides them; (and sometimes by enumerating the types of evidences, then denying them, i.e. denying all the types (by induction), i.e. we have examined them and did not find anything from them here; (and it returns to the first) because its outcome is non-finding (with an extra burden), which is the explanation of the enumeration of the types of evidences, so adhering to the first is preferable to remove this burden. (As for the second), i.e. that everything for which there is no evidence, its negation is obligatory, they establish it in two ways. He pointed to the first by his saying: (For if not for it), i.e. if not for the obligation of negating that for which there is no evidence, (the necessary truths would be negated).
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage defines the two methods to prove that there is no evidence for something, and then states the second principle that everything without evidence must be denied. For the first method, one can either (a) transmit the evidences of those who affirm the thing and demonstrate their weakness, while also confirming that no other evidence exists; or (b) enumerate all possible types of evidence and then deny them entirely through induction, claiming that after exhaustive search nothing was found. The author notes that the second method reduces to the first (since both ultimately rely on non-finding) but with the extra burden of having to enumerate all evidence types, so the first method is preferable. For the second principle—that everything without evidence must be denied—the author introduces a proof: if it were not obligatory to deny what has no evidence, then necessary truths would be negated. This proof is then elaborated in the following lessons.

حصر وجوه الأدلة
الاستقراء
عدم الوجدان
مؤنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The student might think that the two methods are fundamentally different.
Correction: In fact, the second method reduces to the first but with extra work (enumeration). Both ultimately rely on non-finding.

Checkpoint
What are the two methods mentioned to prove that there is no evidence for a thing?
A) Transmitting the evidences of affirmers and showing their weakness; and enumerating all evidence types and denying them.
B) Logical deduction and scriptural proof.
C) Direct perception and rational intuition.
D) Consensus and personal opinion.

Key Takeaway
Proving absence of evidence can be done either by refuting all claimed proofs or by showing no evidence exists through induction, but the first is simpler; also, anything without evidence must be denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned. If you were to apply the first method from this passage, what claimed evidences would you have to gather and refute? Are you willing to do that today?
?
The text warns that without the obligation to negate what lacks evidence, even necessary truths would be negated. In your own life, can you identify one area where you have accepted something without evidence? What necessary truth might be undermined by not applying this principle there?
?
This lesson presents two methods for proving absence of evidence. Which method have you unknowingly used when rejecting others' claims? Now, apply the simpler method (refuting specific evidences) to a personal assumption you have—what do you discover about its foundation?

The Restriction to Theoretical Matters
Sanusi explains the objection and defends the restriction to theoretical matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the principle 'everything without evidence must be denied' must be restricted to theoretical matters (what is not known necessarily) to avoid absurdly denying necessary truths.

Core Idea
Necessary truths are self-evident and thus not subject to the 'no evidence' rule.

A key objection to the principle that everything without evidence must be denied is that it would force us to deny necessary truths (al-ḍarūriyyāt), since there is no evidence for their opposites. Sanusi explains that the principle is correctly restricted to things 'not known necessarily' (ghayr ma'lūm al-thubūt bi al-ḍarūra), i.e., theoretical matters, thereby saving necessary truths. He also defends this restriction and contrasts two methods of argument: invalidating proofs versus enumeration of evidences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing. Notice how Sanusi presents the objection and the defense.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the problem
Why would the unqualified principle lead to denying necessary truths? Because necessary truths have no evidence for their opposites, and the principle says 'no evidence → deny'.
3
Understand the restriction
Sanusi qualifies the principle with 'not known necessarily' (ghayr ma'lūm al-thubūt bi al-ḍarūra). This means the principle only applies to theoretical matters, not self-evident truths.
This avoids absurdity.
4
Examine the defense
Some argued that the restriction should be 'not known necessarily or by reflection', but Sanusi says it's unnecessary because what is known by reflection does have evidence.
He calls this a misunderstanding (wahm).
5
Compare the two methods
Sanusi explains why the method of invalidating proofs is better than enumeration: the real proof is non-finding (ʿadam al-wijdān), and enumeration adds an unnecessary burden.
The lesson's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( غير معلوم الثبوت (بالضرورة المراد بها ما يقابل النظر أي إذا حاولوا نفي شيء نظري الثبوت ولولا التقييد بذلك لانتقض الدليل المذكور بالضروريات لأنه يصدق عليها أنه لا دليل على الضروري وإلا لكان نظرياً، وما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه، فيجب نفي الضروريات وهو ،باطل وما قيل : إنه لو أريد بها ما يقابل النظر لوجب أن يضم إليه أو بالنظر وهم لأن ما علم ثبوته بالنظر لا يصدق عليه أنه لا دليل عليه فما الحاجة إلى الضم .
قوله : ( إذ مآله إلى عدم الوجدان أي مآل الأول إلى عدم الوجدان وإبطال أدلة المثبتين إنما هو لتوقف عدم الوجدان عليه إذ مع صحتهما يشعر عدم الوجدان فالدليل في الحقيقة هو عدم الوجدان بخلاف حصر وجوه الأدلة، إذ لا تعلق ولا توقف لعدم الوجدان عليه، فهو مؤنة زائدة فالأولى تركه والاكتفاء بعدم الوجدان .
قوله: (انتفت الضروريات لأنه لا دليل على خلافها، وإلا لم يكن الضروري علماً فضلاً
His saying: (not known to be established (necessarily) means what is opposite to [knowledge by] reflection, i.e. when they attempt to deny something whose establishment is theoretical. Were it not for this restriction, the mentioned proof would be invalidated by necessary truths, because it is true of them that there is no evidence for the necessary truth—otherwise it would be theoretical—and everything for which there is no evidence must be denied, so necessary truths would have to be denied, which is false. And what is said: that if it meant what is opposite to reflection, then it would have to be joined with 'or by reflection' is a misunderstanding, because what is known to be established by reflection is not truly described as having no evidence for it, so what need is there to join? His saying: (since its outcome is non-finding) i.e. the outcome of the first [method] is non-finding. Invalidation of the evidences of the affirmers is only because non-finding depends on it; for if both [method and result] are sound, non-finding is felt. So the proof in reality is non-finding, unlike the enumeration of the types of evidences, for non-finding has no relation to or dependence on it, so it is an extra burden. So it is better to abandon it and be satisfied with non-finding. His saying: (the necessary truths would be negated) because there is no evidence for their opposite, otherwise the necessary truth would not be knowledge, let alone [being necessary].
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage clarifies the objection that if we accept the principle 'everything without evidence must be denied', then necessary truths (al-daruriyyat) would also be denied, because there is no evidence for their opposite (since they are self-evident). The author restricts the principle to things 'not known to be established necessarily'—i.e., theoretical matters—to avoid this problem. Without this restriction, the principle would apply to necessary truths, and we would have to deny them, which is absurd. The author also explains why the first method (transmitting and refuting evidences) is preferable to enumeration: because the real proof is non-finding, and enumeration adds an unnecessary burden. Finally, the author restates that necessary truths would be negated if we didn't restrict the principle, since there is no evidence for their opposites.

Key Terms
غير معلوم الثبوت بالضرورة
الضروريات
ما يقابل النظر
عدم الوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the principle 'everything without evidence must be denied' applies to all things, including self-evident necessary truths.
Correction: The principle only applies to theoretical matters (what is not known necessarily). Necessary truths are not subject to it because they are self-evident and have no evidence for their opposites.

Checkpoint
Why does the author restrict the principle 'everything without evidence must be denied' to things 'not known necessarily'?
A) To avoid denying necessary truths, which have no evidence for their opposites.
B) Because only theoretical matters can have evidence.
C) Because necessary truths are known by revelation.
D) Because the principle only applies to physical objects.

Key Takeaway
The principle must be restricted to theoretical matters to avoid negating self-evident necessary truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth because you thought it lacked evidence, only to realize it was actually self-evident and foundational? How did that affect your confidence in other beliefs?
?
Sanusi argues that it is better to focus on invalidating false proofs than to list all possible falsehoods. In your own life, do you tend to waste energy enumerating what you reject, or do you actively challenge the evidence for what you oppose? Which approach draws you closer to the truth?
?
The principle 'deny what has no evidence' seems reasonable, but without the restriction, it would destroy necessary truths. What necessary truths (e.g., that God exists, that contradictions are impossible) have you never really examined, assuming they were obvious? Are you willing to test whether you hold them on evidence or just habit?

The Mountain Example and the Collapse of Knowledge
Sanusi illustrates the objection with mountains and extends it to theoretical truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how allowing the existence of things without evidence leads to absurd consequences that negate both necessary and theoretical truths, destroying the possibility of certainty.

Core Idea
Allowing anything without evidence makes all knowledge impossible.

Sanusi now demonstrates the harmful consequences of the opposing principle. He shows that if we accept things without evidence, even basic necessary truths like the absence of mountains in front of us would become uncertain. He then extends this to theoretical reasoning, where unknown counter-evidence or undetected errors would preclude certainty. This passage is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument from necessary truths to theoretical truths.
2
Identify the absurd consequence for necessary truths
Notice how Sanusi uses the example of mountains to show that if we allow existence without evidence, we could doubt that there are no mountains in front of us, which negates necessary truths.
The necessary truth here is our knowledge of the absence of mountains based on perception.
3
Extend to theoretical truths
Then see how the same principle undermines theoretical knowledge: any proof could have an unknown counter-evidence or an error with no evidence, so certainty is impossible.
This shows the principle is self-defeating for all knowledge.
4
Understand the conclusion
Reflect on the final sentence: allowing what has no evidence necessitates objection to the proof. This reinforces why the principle must be rejected.
The lesson is that sound epistemology requires evidence for existence claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( انتفت الضروريات) إذ كل ضروري يصدق على خلافه أنه دليل على ثبوته، كيف ولو كان عليه دليل لم يكن الطرف الذي فرضناه ضرورياً ضرورياً؟ فلو جوز ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه جوز ثبوت خلاف كل ضروري، فانتفت الضروريات بأسرها فإن قلت : المفهوم مما ذكره أولاً أنه لا بد في هذا الطريق من ملاحظة أدلة الثبوت بأحد طريقين، ثم نفيها ولا يكفي مجرد عدم العلم

المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية
۲۳
لجواز أن تكون جبال ) شامخة ( بحضرتنا لا نراها واللام في قوله : ( لعدم الدليل على وجودها متعلقة بالجواز ) والمعنى أنه : إِذا جوز ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه، فحينئذ يجوز أن تكون تلك الجبال بحضرتنا لأنها من قبيل ما لا دليل على ثبوته (و) انتفت (النظريات) أيضاً ( لجواز ) وجود ) معارض للدليل لا نعلمه لعدم ما يدلنا عليه (أو غلط) فيه لا دليل عليه ) والحاصل أنا إذا استدللنا بدليل على حكم نظري، فإن جوزنا ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه جاز أن يكون لذلك الدليل معارض في نفس الأمر لا دليل لنا على وجود ذلك المعارض، فلا نعلمه وجاز أيضاً أن يكون في مقدمات ذلك الدليل غلط لا دليل عليه فلم ينكشف لنا ولا لغيرنا، ومع هذا التجويز لا يمكن حصول اليقين من الدليل فظهر أن تجويز ما لا دليل عليه يوجب القدح
His saying: (the necessary truths would be negated) because for every necessary truth, its opposite is truly described as having evidence for its establishment? How so? And if there were evidence for it, the side we assumed as necessary would not be necessary. So if we allow the establishment of what has no evidence, we allow the establishment of the opposite of every necessary truth, so all necessary truths would be negated. If you say: What is understood from what he mentioned first is that in this way we must consider the evidences of establishment in one of two ways, then deny them, and mere lack of knowledge is not sufficient; because it is possible that [there be] towering mountains in our presence that we do not see. And the lam in his saying: 'for lack of evidence for their existence' is attached to 'permissibility', and the meaning is: if we allow the establishment of what has no evidence, then it is permissible that those mountains be in our presence because they are of the kind for which there is no evidence for its establishment. And (the theoretical truths) would also be negated (because of the possibility of) a counter-evidence to the proof that we do not know due to the lack of something indicating it to us, or an error in it for which there is no evidence. The conclusion: if we reason with a proof for a theoretical judgment, and we allow the establishment of what has no evidence, it is possible that that proof has a counter-evidence in reality for which we have no evidence, so we do not know it; and it is also possible that in the premises of that proof there is an error for which there is no evidence, so it is not disclosed to us or to anyone else. With this allowance, certainty from the proof cannot be obtained. So it is clear that allowing what has no evidence necessitates objection to the proof.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage uses the example of mountains to illustrate the objection against the principle. If we allow that something without evidence can exist, then it would be possible for tall mountains to be right in front of us even though we don't see them, because there is no evidence for their existence. This would negate the necessary truth that we know there are no mountains present. The author then extends the objection to theoretical truths: if we allow the existence of things without evidence, then any proof could have an unknown counter-evidence or an undetected error, because we have no evidence for that counter-evidence or error. Consequently, certainty from proof would be impossible, and all theoretical knowledge would collapse. Thus, the principle must be restricted to prevent these absurd consequences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mountain example is unrealistic because we know mountains aren't there through perception, which is a type of evidence.
Correction: Sanusi's point is that perception itself relies on evidence; if we allow things without any evidence, then even perception could be mistaken. The example shows the absurdity of the principle, not that it is actually possible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence of allowing the existence of things without evidence for necessary truths?
A) Only some necessary truths would be negated.
B) All necessary truths would be negated.
C) Necessary truths would become theoretical truths.
D) Only the mountain example would be affected.

Key Takeaway
Allowing existence without evidence leads to the absurd conclusion that all necessary and theoretical knowledge is uncertain, thus the principle is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

According to this lesson, if you allow the existence of what has no evidence, you are permitting the collapse of all certainty. Where in your life have you allowed doubts to creep in without real evidence, and how has that affected the firmness of your beliefs?
?
Consider your belief in the unseen, like angels or the Day of Judgment. This lesson argues that without evidence, anything could be doubted. But you have evidence from revelation. Question: Are you holding onto that evidence with certainty, or do you allow your mind to entertain possibilities without evidence, thereby weakening your certainty?
?
The lesson shows that allowing error without evidence destroys certainty. When you read the Quran or listen to a scholar, do you automatically suspect error without evidence? How does that undermine your ability to gain knowledge? Commit today to not doubt without evidence.

Refining the Principle: Not All Things Without Evidence Must Be Denied
Sanusi refines the logical rule to account for necessary truths and proper method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refines the principle of denying things without evidence to avoid contradicting necessary truths, and why the absence of evidence must be proven, not assumed.

Core Idea
The absence of evidence must be proven, not assumed.

Sanusi tackles a potential objection to the principle that everything without evidence must be denied. He shows that necessary truths are self-evident and not subject to this rule, and that for theoretical truths, the principle only holds when we have exhaustively searched for evidence. This passage sharpens the epistemological foundation of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall the principle discussed previously: 'everything without evidence must be denied.' The objection is that this would negate necessary truths. Read the passage to see how Sanusi responds.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: it begins with a clarification about necessary truths, then an argument about theoretical truths, then an objection and response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
3
Distinguish necessary truths
Notice that Sanusi says necessary truths are self-evident and not subject to reasoning. There is no evidence for their opposite in reality, so the principle does not apply to them.
This avoids the first objection.
4
Understand the argument for theoretical truths
Sanusi argues that if we allowed the existence of anything without evidence, then theoretical truths would be undermined because counter-evidence or error could be possible. The principle must be applied with caution.
Theoretical truths are established by proof; they are not arbitrary.
5
Grasp the final clarification
The objection that the principle might not require denying everything is answered: when the absence of evidence does not stem from an actual cause of nonexistence, allowance follows universally. The proper method is needed.
Sanusi emphasizes that the principle is valid only when combined with thorough investigation (the two methods).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كون الضروري موضوع الحكم للاستدلال من أن الضروري ليس مما ينظر فيه، بل هو بديهي. على أنه لا دليل على خلافه في نفس الأمر، فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال عليه بعدم الوجدان بأحد الطريقين. قوله : ( وانتفت النظريات) لأنه لا دليل على وجود المعارض لأدلتها، وعلى وجود الغلط في نفس الأمر، وإلا لم تكن تلك النظريات علوماً، فلو جوزنا ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه لجاز ثبوت المعارض لها، والغلط في مقدماتها فلا تكون النظريات علوماً . بالدليل، وحينئذ يتجه أن خلاف كل ضروري ليس مما يعلم انتفاء دليل ثبوته على أحد الوجهين حتى يصح أن يقال : هو من قبيل ما لا دليل على ثبوته بالمعنى المتنازع فيه، فيجوز ثبوته فينتفي نقيضه وهو الضروري قلت خلاف كل ضروري وإن كان لا يتأتي فيه نقل أدلة المثبتين، وبيان ضعفها لعدم مثبت خلاف الضروري في الأكثر ، لكن لا يتأتي فيه حصر وجوه الأدلة ثم نفيها كما لا يخفى مثلاً الضروري في مثال الجبال انتفاؤها بحضرتنا، وخلافه وجودها ووجوه أدلتها رؤيتها مع سلامة الآلات، وحصول الشرائط المعتبرة وحيلولتها بيننا وبين ما وراءها ونحو ذلك فإن قلت انتفاء الضروريات بأسرها إنما يلزم إذا لزم أن كل ما لا دليل عليه جوز إثباته، ولم يلزم هذا لأن انتفاء قوله كل ما لا دليل عليه يجب انتفاؤه بأحد الوجهين، أحدهما أن كل ما لا دليل عليه يجوز إثباته، والثاني أن بعضه يجب انتفاؤه، وبعضه يجوز إثباته فعلى هذا لا يلزم ذلك المحذور ، قلت : انتفاء دليل الثبوت إذا لم يكن منشأ لوجود النفي يلزم جواز الإثبات في الكل، إذ الكلام فيما لا دليل على النفي إلا عدم دليل الثبوت كما لا يخفى، فلا وجه لوجوب النفي في البعض فتأمل.
From the fact that the necessary truth is the subject matter for reasoning argumentation that the necessary truth is not something to be considered by reflection, but is intuitive. Rather, there is no evidence for its opposite in reality, so there is no need to reason about it by non-finding in either of the two ways. His saying: (and the theoretical truths would be negated) because there is no evidence for the existence of a counter-evidence to their proofs, and for the existence of error in reality; otherwise those theoretical truths would not be sciences. So if we allowed the establishment of what has no evidence, it would be allowed that the counter-evidence to them and error in their premises exist, so theoretical truths would not be sciences. By the proof, then it is evident that the opposite of every necessary truth is not something whose evidence of establishment is known to be absent in either of the two ways, so that it can be said: it is of the kind for which there is no evidence for its establishment in the disputed sense, so its establishment is allowed and its contrary, the necessary truth, is negated. I say: The opposite of every necessary truth, even if the transmission of the evidences of the affirmers and showing their weakness is not possible because there is no affirmer of the opposite of the necessary truth in most cases, yet the enumeration of the types of evidences and then denying them is not possible, as is clear. For example, the necessary truth in the example of the mountains is their absence in our presence, and its opposite is their existence, and the types of evidences for it are seeing them with sound faculties, fulfillment of the considered conditions, and their barrier between us and what is behind them, and so on. If you say: The negation of all necessary truths would only follow if it were necessary that everything for which there is no evidence is allowed in its establishment; but this does not follow because the negation of his statement 'everything for which there is no evidence must be negated' has two aspects: one that everything for which there is no evidence is allowed in its establishment, and the second that some of it must be negated and some is allowed in its establishment; so on this, that objection does not follow. I say: If the absence of evidence for establishment does not give rise to existence of negation, then allowance of establishment follows in all, because the discussion is about what has no evidence for negation except the absence of evidence for establishment, as is clear, so there is no justification for obligation of negation in some. So reflect.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage refines the principle to address the objection about necessary and theoretical truths. The author clarifies that necessary truths are self-evident and do not require reasoning; there is no evidence for their opposite in reality. Therefore, the principle 'everything without evidence must be denied' does not apply to them because their opposite is not a candidate for evidence-based denial. For theoretical truths, the objection would arise if we allowed the existence of whatever has no evidence, but the author argues that the lack of evidence for a counter-evidence or error does not automatically make those things possible; rather, the principle must be understood as applying only when there is a genuine absence of evidence for existence, not mere ignorance. The author then addresses a potential counter: that the principle might not require denial of all things without evidence, but only some. He refutes this by arguing that if lack of evidence does not stem from an actual cause of nonexistence, then allowance follows universally. Thus, the proper understanding is that the principle is valid only when combined with thorough investigation (the two methods), not based on simple ignorance.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any lack of evidence justifies denial of a claim.
Correction: The author shows that the absence of evidence must be established through exhaustive methods (the two ways), not merely by ignorance.

Checkpoint

A) It applies only to empirical objects.

D) It applies only to necessary truths.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi clarifies that the principle 'everything without evidence must be denied' applies only when the absence of evidence is established through rigorous methodology, not mere ignorance.

Undermining Necessary Truths: The Reductio Argument
Argues that allowing existence without proof undermines necessary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how permitting existence without proof leads to the negation of necessary truths, and why this argument is a depiction of logical consequence rather than a proof of actual existence.

Core Idea
Permitting the unproven unravels all necessity.

This passage from a classical kalam work presents a crucial reductio ad absurdum: if we allow that something can exist without any proof, then even our most certain truths become uncertain. The author carefully distinguishes between depicting a logical consequence and claiming actual existence, answering a potential objection and concluding on the proper method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Pay attention to the technical terms like جواز, لا دليل عليه, and ضروري.
The passage uses a compressed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Analyze the reductio argument
The argument shows that if we permit the existence of what has no proof, then even the non-existence of mountains before us becomes uncertain, undermining necessary truths.
This is a typical kalam method of showing the consequences of a principle.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author clarifies that this is a depiction of a necessary consequence (luzum), not a proof that the contrary actually exists. This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
The objection that negating one necessary truth doesn't negate all is anticipated and addressed.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that there is no need to rely on the two methods of non-finding, contrary to what some have assumed. Reflect on how this strengthens the argument against permitting existence without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللام في قوله: (لعدم الدليل على وجودها متعلقة بالجواز) والمعنى أنه: إذا جوز ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه، فحينئذ يجوز أن تكون تلك الجبال بحضرتنا لأنها من قبيل ما لا دليل على ثبوته. عن كونه ضرورياً، فلو جوز ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه لجاز ثبوت خلافها فلم تكن الضروريات ضروریات. فقوله لجواز أن تكون إلخ تصوير للزوم انتفاء الضروريات في ضروري معين لا إثبات له، حتى يرد أنه لا يلزم من انتفاء ضرورية هذا الجزئي. انتفاء الضروريات كلها، وبما حررنا لك ظهر أنه لا دليل على خلاف الضروريات في نفس الأمر، فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال عليه بعدم الوجدان بأحد الطريقين المذكورين على ما وهم.
And the lam in his statement: (for lack of proof of its existence is attached to permission) meaning: if one permits the existence of what has no proof, then it is permissible that those mountains be in our presence, because they are of the category of things with no proof of their existence. [This leads to] it not being necessary, for if one permitted the existence of what has no proof, then the existence of its contrary would be permitted, and thus necessary truths would not be necessary. So his statement 'for the permissibility of...' etc. is a depiction of the necessary consequence of the negation of necessary truths in a particular necessary truth, not a proof of it, so that it might be objected that the negation of this particular necessary truth does not necessitate the negation of all necessary truths. And by what we have written for you, it is clear that there is no proof for the contrary of necessary truths in reality, so there is no need to prove it by non-finding in either of the two mentioned ways, as was mistakenly thought.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of an argument against the principle that 'what has no proof may exist'. It begins by explaining the preceding text: the particle 'lam' in 'li-`adam al-dalil' is attached to 'al-jawaz' (permission). The meaning is: if we allow the existence of something for which there is no proof, then it is possible that there are huge mountains right in front of us that we do not see, because they are an example of something with no proof. This would undermine necessary truths, because if we allow the existence of what has no proof, then the opposite of a necessary truth could exist, making it no longer necessary. The author clarifies that this is a depiction of the necessary consequence (luzum) for a specific necessary truth, not a proof that it actually occurs, so one might object that the negation of one particular necessary truth does not entail the negation of all necessary truths. He then concludes that, as established, there is no proof for the contrary of necessary truths in reality, so there is no need to prove it via the two methods of non-finding, contrary to what some have assumed.

Key Terms

لا دليل عليه
ضروري
الضرويات
لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that mountains could actually exist before us unseen.
Correction: It only shows the logical consequence of the principle; it does not claim actual existence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the consequence of permitting the existence of what has no proof for necessary truths?
A) It proves that necessary truths are false.
B) It shows that necessary truths could be false, thus they would not be necessary.
C) It has no effect on necessary truths.
D) It only applies to mountains.

Key Takeaway
Permitting existence without proof logically implies that necessary truths could be false, but this is a depiction of necessity's fragility, not a proof of actual impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed something to be true without proof, treating it as a necessary truth in your life? What would it mean for your faith if that assumption were actually false? Commitment: Identify one such belief today and investigate its foundation.
?
The reductio shows that permitting the unproven unravels certainty. In what areas of your life do you allow unproven assumptions to dictate your actions? Will you let the logic of this argument strip away those false certainties and build your knowledge on proof?
?
If the mountains could be in your presence without evidence, what else could be true without evidence? How does the comfort of unexamined beliefs hinder your pursuit of truth? Resolve to confront one comfortable but unproven belief this week and test it against revelation and reason.

Theoretical Sciences Without Proof
Shows that permitting existence without proof also invalidates theoretical knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why permitting existence without proof undermines the certainty of theoretical sciences.

Core Idea
If you allow the unproven, you lose all certainty in knowledge.

Sanusi now extends the argument to theoretical sciences, which rely on proofs. If we permit what has no proof, then every proof becomes vulnerable to unknown objections or hidden errors, making certainty impossible. This passage shows the devastating consequence for all rational inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation together to grasp the flow. Notice how the author builds from a conditional to a conclusion.
Focus on the logical structure: if X, then Y, therefore Z.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage mentions two things that could undermine a theoretical proof: an unknown objection (معارض) and an unknown error (غلط). Understand why each destroys certainty.
3
Connect to the previous lesson
Recall that the principle 'permitting what has no proof' was already shown to be impossible for necessary truths. Here it is applied to theoretical truths.
This shows the principle is universal.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence states that if we allow the unproven, theoretical sciences would not be sciences at all. Consider why this would be catastrophic for knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) انتفت (النظريات) أيضاً ( لجواز ) وجود ) معارض للدليل لا نعلمه لعدم ما يدلنا عليه (أو غلط) فيه لا دليل عليه ) والحاصل أنا إذا استدللنا بدليل على حكم نظري، فإن جوزنا ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه جاز أن يكون لذلك الدليل معارض في نفس الأمر لا دليل لنا على وجود ذلك المعارض، فلا نعلمه وجاز أيضاً أن يكون في مقدمات ذلك الدليل غلط لا دليل عليه فلم ينكشف لنا ولا لغيرنا، ومع هذا التجويز لا يمكن حصول اليقين من الدليل فظهر أن تجويز ما لا دليل عليه يوجب القدح. قوله: ( وانتفت النظريات) لأنه لا دليل على وجود المعارض لأدلتها، وعلى وجود الغلط في نفس الأمر، وإلا لم تكن تلك النظريات علوماً، فلو جوزنا ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه لجاز ثبوت المعارض لها، والغلط في مقدماتها فلا تكون النظريات علوماً.
And theoretical sciences are also negated because of the permissibility of the existence of an objection to the proof that we do not know due to lack of evidence indicating it to us, or an error in it for which there is no proof. The conclusion is that when we argue with a proof for a theoretical ruling, if we permit the existence of what has no proof, it is possible that there is an objection to that proof in reality for which we have no proof of its existence, so we do not know it, and it is also possible that there is an error in the premises of that proof for which there is no proof, so it is not uncovered to us or to others, and with this permissibility, certainty cannot be obtained from the proof. So it is clear that permitting what has no proof necessitates undermining. His statement: 'And theoretical sciences are negated' because there is no proof of the existence of an objection to their proofs, and of the existence of error in reality, otherwise those theoretical sciences would not be sciences. So if we permitted the existence of what has no proof, then the existence of an objection to them and error in their premises would be permitted, and thus theoretical sciences would not be sciences.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage continues the argument, now showing the effect on theoretical sciences (nazariyyat). It explains that if we allow the existence of something for which there is no proof, then when we have a proof for a theoretical judgment, it becomes possible that there exists an objection to that proof in reality that we are unaware of (because we have no proof of its existence). Similarly, it is possible that there is an error in the premises of the proof, also with no proof pointing to it. Since these possibilities exist, we cannot attain certainty from the proof. The author then comments on the text, stating that theoretical sciences are negated because there is no proof of the existence of an objection to their proofs or of error in reality; otherwise they would not be sciences. Therefore, permitting what has no proof would permit objections and errors, making theoretical sciences impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to necessary truths, not theoretical ones.
Correction: The passage explicitly applies the argument to theoretical sciences, showing that the same principle undermines all areas of knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does permitting existence without proof undermine theoretical sciences?
A) Because it allows for unseen objections or errors, making certainty impossible.
B) Because it proves that all theoretical proofs are false.
C) Because it only affects necessary truths.
D) Because it assumes errors are always present.

Key Takeaway
Permitting what has no proof destroys the possibility of certainty in theoretical sciences because unknown objections or errors could always exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith have you settled for 'probably true' instead of seeking the certainty that comes from proof? Are you willing to examine that area today, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
When you affirm your creed, do you truly believe that no hidden objection or error could undermine it? If you have allowed yourself to believe without evidence, how will you answer Allah when He asks why you did not seek certainty?
?
Have you ever avoided questioning a belief because you feared finding a hidden flaw? This lesson shows that ignoring the possibility of error only destroys certainty. Will you commit to seeking proofs until your faith is built on certainty, not on assumption?

Infinity Objection and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the objection that 'what has no proof' is infinite, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that 'things without proof are infinite' is refuted by distinguishing between 'no proof in reality' and 'no proof in your knowledge'.

Core Idea
The objection collapses because 'no proof' can mean two very different things.

This passage presents a crucial objection against relying on proofs for God's existence: if we accept things without proof, we would have to accept an infinite number of things, which is impossible. Sanusi provides a crisp refutation by splitting the meaning of 'no proof' into two senses, showing the absurd consequences of each.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objection's force sink in before looking at the answer.
The objection claims that allowing 'no proof' leads to an infinite regress.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Sanusi asks: when you say 'no proof', do you mean in reality (في نفس الأمر) or in your knowledge (عندكم)? Find these two phrases in the Arabic text.
The entire refutation hinges on this distinction.
3
Understand the consequences of each
For the first meaning, Sanusi denies that lack of proof in reality is established. For the second, he shows it leads to absurdities about common people and disbelievers.
This step is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإن ما لا دليل عليه من الأشياء غير متناه، فلو جوزنا ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه لزمنا تجويز إثبات ما لا يتناهى وإثباته محال. والجواب أن قولكم في شيء معين أنه لا دليل عليه، إما أن تريدوا به في نفس الأمر أو عدمه عندكم، فإن أردتم الأول قلنا: عدم الدليل على ذلك الشيء في نفس الأمر ممنوع، فإن تزييفكم أدلة المثبتين وعدم وجدانكم بالاستقراء دليلاً عليه لا يفيدان ذلك، لجواز أن يكون هناك دليل لم يطلع عليه أحد، ولئن سلم فعدم الدليل في نفس الأمر لا يدل على عدم ذلك الشيء في نفسه، فإن الصانع تعالى لو لم يوجد العالم لم يدل ذلك على عدمه قطعاً. وإن أردتم الثاني فنقول: عدم الدليل عندكم لا يفيد ولا يدل على عدم ذلك الشيء في نفس الأمر، وإلا لزم علم العوام وكونهم جازمين عالمين بانتفاء الأمور التي لا يعلمون دليلاً على ثبوتها، وعلم الكفار المنكرين لوجود الصانع وتوحيده والنبوة والحشر، أعني يلزم كونهم عالمين بانتفاء هذه الأمور التي ليست عندهم أدلتها، ولزم أن يكون الأجهل بالدلائل أكثر علماً لأن جهله بدليل أي شيء كان دليل له يوصله إلى العلم.
And also: things for which there is no proof are infinite. So if we permitted the existence of what has no proof, we would be forced to permit the existence of the infinite, and its existence is impossible. The answer: your statement concerning a specific thing that there is no proof for it, either you mean in reality or your lack of it. If you mean the first, we say: the lack of proof for that thing in reality is denied, for your falsifying the proofs of the affirmers and your not finding by induction a proof for it do not establish that, because it is possible that there is a proof that no one has discovered. Even if it is granted, the lack of proof in reality does not prove the non-existence of that thing in itself; for if the Maker, exalted, did not create the world, that would not prove His non-existence, absolutely. And if you mean the second, we say: the lack of proof with you does not prove nor does it indicate the non-existence of that thing in reality; otherwise, it would necessitate that common people are knowers, being certain and knowing the non-existence of things for which they do not know a proof of their existence, and that the disbelievers who deny the existence of the Maker, His unity, prophecy, and resurrection are knowers—I mean it would necessitate that they are knowers of the non-existence of these things for which they do not have proofs. And it would necessitate that the most ignorant of proofs is most knowing, because his ignorance of a proof for anything is a proof leading him to knowledge.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection and its refutation. The objection is: things with no proof are infinite. If we allow the existence of what has no proof, we would be allowing the existence of the infinite, which is impossible. The refutation distinguishes two meanings of 'there is no proof for a thing': either in reality itself, or in your own knowledge. If the former, it is denied because even if you falsify others' proofs and fail to find a proof by induction, there might still be a proof unknown to anyone. Moreover, even if we grant that there is no proof in reality, that does not imply the thing's non-existence, as illustrated by the Maker: His non-creation of the world does not prove His non-existence. If the latter (meaning in your knowledge), then lack of proof with you does not prove non-existence in reality; otherwise, it would lead to absurd consequences: common people would be considered knowers of the non-existence of things they have no proof for, disbelievers would be knowers of the non-existence of God, prophethood, etc., and the most ignorant of proofs would be the most knowledgeable, because their ignorance itself would serve as proof. These absurdities show the invalidity of that interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'no proof' means the same in reality and in our knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the lack of proof in reality does not imply non-existence, and lack of proof in your knowledge certainly does not.

Checkpoint
What is one absurd consequence if 'no proof' means 'no proof in your knowledge' and that proves non-existence?
A) Disbelievers would be correct.
B) Common people would be knowers of the non-existence of many things.
C) The infinite would exist.
D) Proofs would be unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The objection that things without proof lead to an infinite regress is refuted by distinguishing the sense of 'no proof' and showing its absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief or truth claim simply because you personally lacked proof for it? What does this passage reveal about the arrogance of assuming your own knowledge is the measure of reality? Will you now hold your judgments more cautiously?
?
The disbeliever who denies God, prophethood, and resurrection is not considered a knower of their non-existence, even though he lacks proof for them. Are there areas in your life where you have passively accepted negation—believing something false—because you never sought evidence? What specific step can you take today to actively seek proof rather than assume absence?
?
If the most ignorant person would be the most knowledgeable under the false assumption that lack of proof proves non-existence, then what does your own ignorance reveal about you? When you encounter something you have no proof for, will you humbly admit ignorance or arrogantly deny? Make a concrete commitment: This week, when you face a matter of faith you doubt, will you research it or let the doubt fester?

6 min
The Two Meanings of 'No Proof' and Their Refutation
Commentary refuting the impossibility-of-absence-of-proof argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the argument that 'what has no proof is impossible' by distinguishing two senses of 'no proof' and showing the fallacy in each.

Core Idea
Absence of proof is not proof of absence.

The objection that 'what has no proof is infinite and therefore impossible' is a common skeptical challenge. The commentator first clarifies that the phrase 'no proof' can mean either no proof in reality or no proof in the objector's own knowledge. Each meaning is addressed separately, revealing the argument's flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to see the structure: the objection is stated, then the answer begins with a distinction between two senses of 'no proof'. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice how the answer immediately splits into two cases.
2
Understand the first sense
The first sense is 'no proof in reality'. The commentator denies that such a state can be known, and even if granted, it does not imply the thing's nonexistence. Ponder the analogy with God's creation.
3
Understand the second sense
The second sense is 'no proof in your view'. The commentator argues that this also does not imply negation, otherwise absurd consequences follow. Identify the two examples given.
4
Reflect on the consequences
The passage lists three absurdities: common people would be knowledgeable, disbelievers would be knowledgeable, and the most ignorant would be most knowledgeable. Ask yourself why these are unacceptable.
This shows the reductio ad absurdum method.

For if the ignorant person's belief in their negation due to the absence of proof in his view becomes knowledge, then the knowledgeable person's belief in their existence would be ignorance. So the most ignorant of proofs has more abundant knowledge of things. And in Nihayat al-Uqul, a proof may occur in the future, such as the informant of the Law informing about what is only known through his information concerning the states of Paradise and Hell, and the amounts of reward and punishment. So the absence of proof in reality nor its absence in our view necessitates the negation of the indicated thing in itself. His saying: (Then the knowledgeable person's belief in its existence would be ignorance, etc.) Because it is assumed that what has no proof for a person must be negated. So if the knowledgeable person's belief were not ignorance, it would necessitate the truth of two contradictories, and if one considers that the knowledgeable person's belief is knowledge in reality, it would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictories. His saying: (And in Nihayat al-Uqul, etc.) is an indication that the wording of the text can be interpreted as invalidating both sides. His saying: (For the ignorant person's belief in its negation due to the absence of proof in his view becomes knowledge, etc.) There is investigation in it, because it is known from what he mentioned at the beginning of this method that it is necessary to consider the proofs of affirmation in one of the two ways, then negate them as we stated previously, and it is not sufficient to simply lack awareness of proofs. So in these cases, i.e., where the knowledgeable person knows a proof for the existence of something, the absence of proof in the ignorant person is not established, so that his belief in the negation of that thing becomes knowledge. Rather, it is established if he observes the knowledgeable person's affirming proof and refutes it in reality, and this refutation is not possible in reality, otherwise the affirmer would not be knowledgeable. And it may be answered that this is talk based on the premise, because his saying 'otherwise it would necessitate' is in the force of a premise, so the denial remains. But you may say: What is meant by the knowledgeable person's knowledge of affirmation is his belief that corresponds to reality, and this belief may arise from a weak proof, like the weak proofs of the people of truth. So if the ignorant person's refutation of this weak proof benefits him knowledge in the sense of corresponding belief, then the knowledgeable person's belief becomes ignorance not corresponding to reality, so the statement is complete. Then the claim that the knowledgeable person's belief becomes ignorance because the ignorant person's belief is knowledge is a precise statement, for it is not probable that both beliefs are knowledge in the sense of their correspondence to reality. So it cannot be objected that this is only valid if the speaker using the mentioned method denies that what is based on knowledge of the proof is knowledge, which is obviously false because it necessitates the negation of the Maker and His oneness, etc. This is because what is meant is the necessity of this absurdity in reality, not its commitment. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on an objection-and-answer argument concerning the claim that 'what has no proof is infinite and therefore impossible.' The commentator explains the argument and provides a detailed refutation. The passage begins by restating the objection: if we permit the affirmation of what has no proof, we would have to affirm the infinite, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes two senses of 'no proof': in reality or in the view of the interlocutor. If the first, the commentator denies that there is no proof in reality, because the absence of proof found by the objector does not mean there is no proof at all. Even if granted, absence of proof does not imply absence of the thing itself, using the analogy that God's not creating the world would not imply His nonexistence. If the second sense (no proof in one's view), the commentator argues that this does not necessitate negation, because otherwise it would lead to absurdities: common people would be considered knowledgeable about negations they cannot prove, and disbelievers would be considered knowledgeable in denying God, prophethood, and resurrection. Furthermore, the most ignorant person would be the most knowledgeable, since his ignorance of proof becomes his proof. The commentator then clarifies the meaning of 'infinite' and the necessary consequence: if what has no proof is infinite, the collective of such things is infinite, and permitting one leads to permitting the infinite. But he notes that this reasoning is not straightforward, because things being among the infinite does not automatically permit the infinite; it only permits each individually, and philosophers even allow infinite regress in causes. The commentator then discusses the second point: 'absence of proof in your view' cannot mean in the view of all rational beings, as that would make the method impossible. He explains the basis of inference is lack of finding and refutation of proofs. The passage then revisits the first point, stating that the interpretation suits the answer and that proving the infinity of what has no proof is by observation. On the point about commoners, the commentator considers an objection: if 'absence of proof' means in everyone's view, the method is impossible. The commentary continues to page 25, discussing that if absence of proof in one's view necessitates negation, then the ignorant person would equal the knowledgeable in matters without proof, and the ignorant would be more knowledgeable in matters where the knowledgeable has proof, leading to the absurdity that the most ignorant is most knowledgeable. It also notes that knowledge of proof may arise in the future, so current ignorance does not yield certainty. The example from Nihayat al-Uqul is given: revelation can provide proofs about the afterlife that were unknown before, so absence of proof does not necessitate negation. Finally, the commentator refines the analysis, arguing that the ignorant person's belief becomes knowledge only if he has considered and refuted the proof, not merely by lacking awareness. He discusses different interpretations and concludes that the argument is precise and can be used in debate.

Key Terms
ما لا دليل عليه
عدم الدليل في نفس الأمر
عدم الدليل عندكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something has no proof found by me, then it must be false or impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that absence of proof in one's view does not entail nonexistence, because proof may exist that one is unaware of, or the thing may be true despite lack of evidence known to the person.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main flaw in the argument that 'what has no proof is impossible'?
A) The infinite cannot be proven to be impossible.
B) The argument relies on an ambiguous understanding of 'no proof', which can mean either 'in reality' or 'in one's view', and neither implies impossibility.
C) The argument ignores the possibility that the objector is wrong.
D) The argument is valid only for necessary matters.

Key Takeaway
The key refutation: absence of proof, whether in reality or in one's knowledge, does not logically imply the nonexistence of the thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you personally lack evidence for a truth claim, do you automatically dismiss it? How does this passage challenge you to hold a humbler stance before concluding nonexistence?
?
The passage warns that assuming 'no proof in my view' equals nonexistence leads to absurdities like disbelievers being 'knowledgeable'. In your own life, what beliefs have you rejected simply because you never investigated them? Will you examine one such belief this week?
?
Think of a time you were certain something didn't exist because you had never seen proof. Could it still be true? What if God holds you accountable for that certainty? What step will you take to seek proof before assuming absence?

Absurd Consequences and the Infinity Analysis
Analysis of the absurd consequences and the infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the absurd consequences that follow from the claim that 'what has no proof is impossible' and the nuanced analysis of the infinity premise.

Core Idea
If absence of proof meant impossibility, the ignorant would be the most knowledgeable.

After showing the initial fallacy, the commentator deepens the critique by examining what happens if we accept the claim. The argument would imply that common people and disbelievers are knowledgeable about theological truths, and that the more ignorant a person is of proofs, the more they know. Additionally, the premise that what has no proof is infinite is analyzed and shown to be not necessarily valid.

Guided Path
1
Explore the absurd consequences
Read the passage focusing on the reductio ad absurdum: if 'no proof in your view' meant the thing is false, then commoners and disbelievers would be considered knowledgeable about negations they cannot prove. Ask yourself why this is absurd.
2
Examine the infinity premise
The commentator points out that the inference from 'what has no proof is infinite' to 'affirming the infinite is impossible' is not straightforward. Notice the nuance: the totality of such things might be infinite, but each individual thing is not automatically impossible. Philosophers even allow infinite regress in causes.
3
Consider future proofs
The passage mentions that proofs may arise in the future (e.g., from revelation). This shows that current absence of proof does not guarantee future absence, so one cannot be certain of negation based on present ignorance.
This connects to the example from Nihayat al-Uqul.

Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on an objection-and-answer argument concerning the claim that 'what has no proof is infinite and therefore impossible.' The commentator explains the argument and provides a detailed refutation. The passage begins by restating the objection: if we permit the affirmation of what has no proof, we would have to affirm the infinite, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes two senses of 'no proof': in reality or in the view of the interlocutor. If the first, the commentator denies that there is no proof in reality, because the absence of proof found by the objector does not mean there is no proof at all. Even if granted, absence of proof does not imply absence of the thing itself, using the analogy that God's not creating the world would not imply His nonexistence. If the second sense (no proof in one's view), the commentator argues that this does not necessitate negation, because otherwise it would lead to absurdities: common people would be considered knowledgeable about negations they cannot prove, and disbelievers would be considered knowledgeable in denying God, prophethood, and resurrection. Furthermore, the most ignorant person would be the most knowledgeable, since his ignorance of proof becomes his proof. The commentator then clarifies the meaning of 'infinite' and the necessary consequence: if what has no proof is infinite, the collective of such things is infinite, and permitting one leads to permitting the infinite. But he notes that this reasoning is not straightforward, because things being among the infinite does not automatically permit the infinite; it only permits each individually, and philosophers even allow infinite regress in causes. The commentator then discusses the second point: 'absence of proof in your view' cannot mean in the view of all rational beings, as that would make the method impossible. He explains the basis of inference is lack of finding and refutation of proofs. The passage then revisits the first point, stating that the interpretation suits the answer and that proving the infinity of what has no proof is by observation. On the point about commoners, the commentator considers an objection: if 'absence of proof' means in everyone's view, the method is impossible. The commentary continues to page 25, discussing that if absence of proof in one's view necessitates negation, then the ignorant person would equal the knowledgeable in matters without proof, and the ignorant would be more knowledgeable in matters where the knowledgeable has proof, leading to the absurdity that the most ignorant is most knowledgeable. It also notes that knowledge of proof may arise in the future, so current ignorance does not yield certainty. The example from Nihayat al-Uqul is given: revelation can provide proofs about the afterlife that were unknown before, so absence of proof does not necessitate negation. Finally, the commentator refines the analysis, arguing that the ignorant person's belief becomes knowledge only if he has considered and refuted the proof, not merely by lacking awareness. He discusses different interpretations and concludes that the argument is precise and can be used in debate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite is impossible, so any argument that leads to affirming the infinite is automatically false.
Correction: The commentator notes that the inference from 'what has no proof is infinite' to 'affirming the infinite is impossible' is not straightforward, and some philosophers even accept infinite regress in certain contexts.

Checkpoint
What example does the commentator give from Nihayat al-Uqul to illustrate that proofs can arise in the future?
A) The existence of a high mountain in one's presence.
B) The informant of the Law revealing details about Paradise and Hell.
C) The philosophers' acceptance of infinite regress.
D) The common people's knowledge of negations.

Key Takeaway
Accepting the claim leads to absurdities that the arguer would not accept, and the logic of the infinite regress is more subtle than assumed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief simply because you couldn’t prove it, without considering that your own knowledge might be limited? How does this lesson challenge you to remain humble about what you don’t know?
?
If you were the one making the claim that 'no proof means false', would you accept that commoners and disbelievers could be the most knowledgeable? How does that absurdity force you to examine your own reasoning patterns?
?
When you encounter a truth claim that you find hard to prove, do you demand absolute certainty before accepting it? This lesson shows that proofs may come later (e.g., through revelation) — how can you cultivate patience and openness to future evidence?

intermediate
