Refuting the Principle that Absence of Evidence Implies Non-Existence
Sanusi refutes the claim that absence of evidence implies non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that lack of evidence for something proves its non-existence is rationally unsound and leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

How do we know that something does not exist? Some argue that if we have no evidence for it, it must be non-existent. But this simple rule leads to paradoxical results. In this passage, the author systematically refutes this principle, showing that it confuses ignorance with knowledge and overlooks future evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Notice the logical outcome: if lack of evidence equals knowledge of non-existence, then the ignorant person becomes more knowledgeable than the knowledgeable one. This exposes the fallacy.
3
Examine the two objections
The author presents two objections: evidence may arise in the future, and some things (like the absence of a mountain before us) are known self-evidently, not by this false premise.
4
Reflect on the core idea
Consider how this refutation is important in theology: not having evidence for God's existence, for example, does not prove His non-existence.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعدم ذلك الشيء، فيساوي الجاهل العالم فيما لا يقيمان عليه دليلاً، ويزداد علم الجاهل فيما علم العالم دليلا على ثبوته، فإن اعتقاد الجاهل بانتفائه لعدم الدليل عنده لما كان علماً كان اعتقاد العالم بثبوته جهلاً، فيكون الأجهل بالدلائل أوفر علماً بالأشياء ( مع أنه ( أي العلم بالدليل ( قد يحدث ) في الاستقبال ومع هذا الاحتمال لا يكون الجهل به في الحال مفيداً لليقين بانتفاء المدلول، وفي نهاية العقول أن الدليل قد يحدث في الاستقبال كإخبار الشارع بما لا يعلم إلا بإخباره من أحوال الجنة والنار، ومقادير الثواب والعقاب، فلا يكون عدم الدليل في نفس الأمر ولا عدمه عندنا مقتضياً لانتفاء المدلول في نفسه ( والعلم بعدم الجبل الشاهق بحضرتنا ضروري لا يتوقف على هذه المقدمة القائلة بأن كل ما لا دليل على ثبوته فإنه يجب انتفاؤه
By the non-existence of that thing, so the ignorant equals the knowledgeable in what they both do not establish evidence for, and the knowledge of the ignorant increases in that for which the knowledgeable found evidence of its existence. For if the ignorant's belief in its non-existence due to lack of evidence with him is knowledge, then the knowledgeable's belief in its existence is ignorance. Thus the one more ignorant of evidences is more abundant in knowledge of things. While that (i.e., knowledge of the evidence) may occur in the future, and with this possibility ignorance of it at present does not yield certainty of the non-existence of the indicated thing. And in Nihayat al-Uqul that evidence may arise in the future, like the informing of the Lawgiver about matters of Paradise and Hell and the amounts of reward and punishment, which are not known except through His informing. Thus neither the absence of evidence in reality nor its absence with us necessitates the non-existence of the indicated thing in itself. And knowledge of the non-existence of a towering mountain in our presence is self-evident and does not depend on this premise that whatever has no evidence for its existence must be non-existent.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that absence of evidence for a thing necessarily implies its non-existence. The author first shows the absurd consequence: if lack of evidence alone were sufficient to judge non-existence, then the ignorant and the knowledgeable would be equal on matters where neither has evidence, and the ignorant's knowledge would increase in cases where the knowledgeable has evidence, making the ignorant more knowledgeable. Then he introduces two key objections: (1) Evidence may arise in the future, so current ignorance does not produce certainty of non-existence, as illustrated by future revelation from the Lawgiver. (2) Some knowledge, like the absence of a mountain before us, is self-evident and does not rely on the false premise that absence of evidence implies non-existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no evidence for a thing, then it must not exist.
Correction: Absence of evidence is not a proof of non-existence; it could be due to ignorance or future evidence. The principle leads to absurd results and is not universally valid.

Checkpoint

A) The knowledgeable person becomes more knowledgeable
B) The ignorant person becomes equal to the knowledgeable in knowledge
C) The ignorant person knows more about things than the knowledgeable person
D) The knowledgeable person is always certain

Key Takeaway
The absence of evidence for something does not rationally entail its non-existence, as this would collapse knowledge into ignorance.

The Argument from Infinity
The author argues that infinite existence can only be judged if a categorical proof exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from infinite existence refutes the claim that absence of evidence for existence entails non-existence, and why analogy from finite to infinite is invalid without a categorical proof.

Core Idea
Infinity breaks the analogy; without categorical proof, possibility remains.

This lesson continues the refutation of the principle that absence of evidence for existence proves non-existence. The author presents a key example: if something infinite is proven impossible, analogy fails; otherwise, its existence cannot be ruled out. This sharpens our understanding of when absence of evidence is and isn't decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct parts: a brief point about necessary premises, then the longer infinity argument.
The parentheses are from the commentary tradition.
2
Identify the two scenarios in the infinity argument
The passage describes two cases: when a categorical proof (qāṭiʿ) prevents infinite existence, analogy fails; otherwise, the judgment of non-existence is not necessary, and existence is possible.
Focus on the conditional structure: 'if... then...; otherwise...'.
3
Connect to the broader refutation
Ask yourself how this argument undermines the principle that absence of evidence for existence implies non-existence. The example shows that without a categorical proof, we cannot rule out existence.
This is a direct attack on the principle's universal applicability.
4
Note the role of self-evident premises
The opening sentence reminds us that knowledge of absence of ignorance is necessary, not theoretical. This reinforces the idea that the principle being refuted is not self-evident.

( وإلا لكان) العلم بعدم الجهل (نظرياً) لا ضرورياً ( وعدم المعارض والغلط في المقدمات القطعية ضرورية كانت أو نظرية ) ضروري ) معلوم بالبديهة، فلا يتوقف على الاستدلال بتلك المقدمة الفاسدة ) ووجود ما لا نهاية له إن امتنع لقاطع) دل على امتناعه ( امتنع القياس عليه ) أعني قياس ما لا دليل عليه من الأمور المتناهية التي لم يدل قاطع على امتناعها، لظهور الفارق حينئذ ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع القاطع ( منع الحكم الذي هو وجوب الانتفاء ( فيه ) أي فيما لا يتناهى، وجوز ثبوته ) في نفس الأمر كسائر الأمور التي لا دليل على ثبوتها، ولا قاطع يدل على امتناعها ( وأيضاً) إن صح ما ذكرتم من أن عدم الدليل على الثبوت، يستلزم العلم بالعدم وجـ أن يكون عدم الدليل على الانتفاء مستلزما للعلم بالثبوت (فيلزم من عدم دليل الطرفين أي الانتفاء والثبوت ( الجزم بهما معا في شيء واحد ( لا يقال : عدم دليل النبوة يدل على عدمها (قطعا ) فإنا إذا لم نجد مع إنسان ما يدل على نبوته، جزمنا بأنه ليس نبياً بلا شبهة بخلاف عدم دليل عدمها فإنا إذا لم نجد معه ما يدل على عدم نبوته، لم نجزم بأنه نبي فليس يلزم من كون عدم دليل الوجود مستلزما للنفي، كون
(Otherwise it would be that) knowledge of the non-existence of ignorance (would be theoretical) not necessary. (And the absence of opposition and error in categorical premises, whether necessary or theoretical, is necessary) known by intuition, so it does not depend on inference by that corrupt premise. (And the existence of what has no end, if it is prevented by a categorical proof) indicating its impossibility, (then analogy upon it is prevented) i.e., analogizing what has no evidence among finite things that no categorical proof indicates their impossibility, because the distinction is clear then. (Otherwise) i.e., if there is no preventing categorical proof, (then the judgment which is necessity of non-existence is prevented) in it, i.e., in what is infinite, and its existence is allowed) in reality like other things for which there is no evidence of existence and no categorical proof indicating their impossibility. (And also) if what you mentioned is correct, that absence of evidence for existence necessitates knowledge of non-existence, then absence of evidence for non-existence should necessitate knowledge of existence. (So from absence of evidence for both sides i.e., non-existence and existence) it follows (to affirm both together in one thing). (It cannot be said: absence of evidence for prophethood indicates its non-existence) definitely, for if we do not find with a person something indicating his prophethood, we judge definitively that he is not a prophet without doubt, unlike absence of evidence for its non-existence, for if we do not find with him something indicating his non-prophethood, we do not judge that he is a prophet. So it does not follow from the fact that absence of evidence for existence necessitates negation, (كون) ...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation, presenting two additional arguments. The first concerns the necessity of self-evident premises: knowledge of the absence of a mountain is necessary, not dependent on the corrupt premise. The second uses the example of infinite existence: if something is proven impossible by a categorical proof, then analogy with finite things fails; otherwise, the judgment of non-existence is not necessary. The third is a mutual implication argument: if absence of evidence for existence entails non-existence, then absence of evidence for non-existence entails existence, leading to affirming both opposites simultaneously. An objection is raised about prophecy: absence of evidence for prophethood gives certainty of non-prophethood, but absence of evidence for non-prophethood does not give certainty of prophethood. The passage ends abruptly at 'كون', which likely begins a response.

Correction: The passage shows that analogy is invalid when a categorical proof prevents infinite existence; otherwise, existence is possible, so the analogy does not hold.

Key Takeaway
The argument from infinity shows that without a categorical proof, the existence of something cannot be ruled out by analogy.

The Mutual Implication Argument and the Prophecy Objection
The author presents a mutual implication argument and an objection concerning prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutual implication argument that demonstrates the absurdity of the principle that absence of evidence for existence entails non-existence, and how the prophecy objection attempts to rescue it.

Core Idea
Mutual implication proves too much; the prophecy objection shows an asymmetry.

The author now presents a devastating reductio: if absence of evidence for existence proves non-existence, then by the same logic absence of evidence for non-existence would prove existence. This would force affirming both opposites when evidence is lacking for both. An objection about prophethood attempts to break the symmetry, but the passage cuts off before the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the mutual implication argument
Read the beginning of the passage where the author sets up the reductio. Notice the logical symmetry: if absence of evidence for X entails not-X, then absence of evidence for not-X should entail X.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the consequence
If evidence is absent for both existence and non-existence, the principle would force us to affirm both simultaneously, which is impossible.
3
Examine the prophecy objection
The objector claims that in the case of prophethood, absence of evidence for prophethood does prove non-prophethood, but absence of evidence for non-prophethood does not prove prophethood. This breaks the symmetry.
The objection is presented as a counterexample to the mutual implication.
4
Note the cut-off
The passage ends abruptly at 'كون', likely beginning the author's response. Consider what the response might be.
This incomplete passage leaves room for discussion.

The Objection of Symmetry and the Prophethood Example
Objection and response concerning symmetry of lack of evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the doctrine that lack of evidence entails negation, and how the response resolves it by distinguishing between lack of evidence and definitive proof.

Core Idea
Lack of evidence does not entail knowledge without a definitive proof.

In this passage, the author addresses a clever objection: if lack of evidence for existence implies non-existence, then by symmetry lack of evidence for non-existence would imply existence, leading to a contradiction. To illustrate, the objector brings up the example of prophethood. But the response shows that our certainty in such cases actually rests on a definitive proof, not on lack of evidence alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Note the structure: objection, attempted counterexample, and response.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if the principle holds symmetrically, we would have to affirm both contradictories. Find that part.
3
Examine the prophethood example
The objector tries to show asymmetry: we judge a person a non-prophet from lack of evidence but not a prophet from lack of evidence against prophethood. How does the response address this?
4
Understand the response's key move
The response argues that the certainty about non-prophethood after Muhammad comes from a definitive proof (that no prophet after Muhammad), not from lack of evidence.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it shows that our rational judgments are not based purely on absence of evidence but on positive grounds.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن صح ما ذكرتم من أن عدم الدليل على الثبوت يستلزم العلم بالعدم فيلزم من عدم دليل الطرفين الجزم بهما معاً في شيء واحد لا يقال عدم دليل النبوة يدل على عدمها قطعاً فإنا إذا لم نجد مع إنسان ما يدل على نبوته جزمنا بأنه ليس نبياً بلا شبهة بخلاف عدم دليل عدمها فإنا إذا لم نجد معه ما يدل على عدم نبوته لم نجزم بأنه نبي فليس يلزم من كون عدم دليل الوجود مستلزماً للنفي كون عدم دليل النفي مستلزماً للوجود حتى يلزم ما ذكرتم من الجزم بالنقيضين معاً لأنا نقول الجزم بعدم نبوته ليس لذلك المدرك الذي هو عدم الدليل على نبوته بل للدليل القاطع الدال على أن لا نبي بعد محمد ولولا هذا القاطع لما جزمنا بعدم نبوته
And also, if what you mentioned is correct—that lack of evidence for existence entails knowledge of non-existence—then it would follow from lack of evidence for both sides that we affirm both simultaneously in one thing. It cannot be said: Lack of evidence for prophethood definitely indicates its non-existence, for when we find no evidence for a person's prophethood, we are certain that he is not a prophet without doubt, unlike lack of evidence for non-prophethood, for when we find no evidence for his non-prophethood, we are not certain that he is a prophet. Thus, it does not follow that lack of evidence for existence implies non-existence entails that lack of evidence for non-existence implies existence, so that what you mentioned of affirming both opposites follows. But we say: The certainty regarding his non-prophethood is not due to that source, which is lack of evidence for his prophethood, but rather due to the definitive proof indicating that there is no prophet after Muhammad. Without this definitive proof, we would not be certain of his non-prophethood.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a response. The objector argues that if lack of evidence for existence implied non-existence, then by symmetry lack of evidence for non-existence would imply existence, leading to contradiction when both are absent. The objector then tries to rebut this by giving the example of prophethood: we judge a person a non-prophet due to lack of evidence for his prophethood, but we do not judge him a prophet due to lack of evidence for his non-prophethood, showing asymmetry. However, the response clarifies that our certainty about a person not being a prophet is not actually based on lack of evidence, but on the definitive proof that there is no prophet after Muhammad. Thus, the example does not support the claim that lack of evidence for non-existence implies existence.

Distinction between lack of evidence for negation and for affirmation
Al-Jurjani refutes an objection about evidence and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between reasoning from lack of evidence for negation versus lack of evidence for affirmation, and why this distinction resolves an apparent paradox about infinite regress.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence—unless you have decisive proof.

This passage tackles a subtle but critical methodological distinction in kalam: the difference between using lack of evidence for negation to prove existence versus using lack of evidence for affirmation to prove non-existence. An objector argues that the former leads to an infinite regress, but the author (al-Jurjani) shows that the two cases are not symmetrical. By understanding this distinction, you will see how sound reasoning avoids contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Carefully read the objection claiming that lack of evidence for negation leads to affirming the infinite, and the initial response distinguishing the two cases.
Notice how the objection tries to force a contradiction.
2
Examine the author's example
Look at the example of certainty about no prophet after Muhammad. The author explains that such certainty comes from decisive evidence, not from lack of evidence for prophethood.
This illustrates the principle in action.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author clarifies that he is not defending the method of reasoning from lack of evidence for negation, but rather showing the equivalence between the two methods in the intellect.
The goal is to demonstrate inconsistency in the objector's position.
4
Analyze the entailment issue
Consider why the entailment between the two methods is not established, and why the claim of no difference is not accepted.
This is the crux of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية
عدم دليل النفي مستلزماً للوجود حتى يلزم ما ذكرتم من الجزم بالنقيضين معاً، وأيضاً يلزم هنا أي من كون عدم دليل النفي مستلزماً للوجود إثبات ما لا يتناهى وهو ممتنع ( و ) يلزم (ثمة ) أي من كون عدم دليل الوجود مستلزماً للانتفاء (نفيه) أي نفي ما لا يتناهى ولا يمتنع هذا النفي فظهر الفرق واندفع الإشكال (لأنا نقول : الجزم بعدم نبوته ) أي نبوة من لا نجد دليلا على نبوته ( ليس لذلك المدرك ) الذي هو عدم الدليل على نبوته بل للدليل القاطع الدال ( على أن لا نبي بعد محمد ( ولولا هذا القاطع لما جزمنا بعدم نبوته ( وأما الثاني) أي الجواب عنه ( فالغرض ) مما ذكرنا ليس هو أن الاستدلال بعدم دليل النفي على الثبوت طريق مستقيم، حتى يتجه علينا أنه يفضي إلى إثبات ما لا يتناهى بل الغرض ( أنه لا فارق بينهما ) أي بين الاستدلال بعدم دليل الثبوت على النفي والاستدلال بعدم دليل النفي على الثبوت ( في العقل) فلو جاز الأول جاز الثاني لكنه ممتنع لوجوه منها ما ذكرتم، من أنه يلزم منه إثبات ما لا يتناهى ( وإنما يتمشى هذا الجواب (لو أثبت الملازمة ) بين جواز الأول وجواز الثاني، لكنها لم تثبت ودعوى عدم الفارق مع ظهوره غير مسموعة .
The Sixth Observation - Fifth Objective: What are the strong methods? [Objection:] 'Lack of evidence for negation necessitates existence, such that it entails what you mentioned of certainty in both contradictories together, and also it entails here, i.e., from the fact that lack of evidence for negation necessitates existence, the establishment of the infinite, which is impossible.' [Answer:] 'And it entails there, i.e., from the fact that lack of evidence for existence necessitates denial, its denial, i.e., the denial of the infinite, and this denial is not impossible. So the difference appears and the difficulty is removed.' [We say:] 'Certainty in the absence of his prophethood, i.e., the prophethood of one for whom we find no evidence of his prophethood, is not due to that source, i.e., the lack of evidence for his prophethood, but rather due to decisive evidence indicating that there is no prophet after Muhammad. And were it not for this decisive evidence, we would not be certain of the absence of his prophethood. As for the second, i.e., the answer to it, the purpose of what we mentioned is not that reasoning from lack of evidence for negation to affirmation is a sound method, such that it would be directed at us that it leads to establishing the infinite; rather, the purpose is that there is no difference between them, i.e., between reasoning from lack of evidence for affirmation to denial and reasoning from lack of evidence for negation to affirmation, in the intellect. So if the first were permissible, the second would be permissible. But it is impossible for reasons including what you mentioned, that it would entail establishing the infinite. And this answer only proceeds if the entailment is established between the permissibility of the first and the permissibility of the second, but it has not been established, and the claim of no difference, despite its obviousness, is not to be heard.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against a previous argument. The objector had claimed that the method of using lack of evidence for negation to prove existence leads to affirming the infinite, which is impossible. The author distinguishes between two cases: lack of evidence for negation and lack of evidence for affirmation. The objection is that if lack of evidence for negation proves existence, then many infinite things would be proven, which is impossible. The answer is that lack of evidence for affirmation, on the other hand, only entails denial of existence, which is not impossible. The author then gives an example: we are certain that no one after Muhammad is a prophet, not because we lack evidence for their prophethood, but because we have decisive evidence that there is no prophet after him. The author further clarifies that the purpose is not to defend the method of reasoning from lack of evidence for negation to affirmation as a sound path, but rather to show that there is no rational difference between that method and the method of reasoning from lack of evidence for affirmation to denial. If one were permissible, the other would be too, but the latter is impossible, so the former is also impossible. However, this answer only works if the entailment between the two methods is established, but it is not. The claim that there is no difference is not accepted given the obvious difference.

Key Terms
عدم دليل النفي
عدم دليل الثبوت
ما لا يتناهى
دليل قاطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author is defending reasoning from lack of evidence for negation to affirmation as a valid method.
Correction: The author is actually showing that both methods are equivalent in the intellect, and since one is impossible, both are; he is not endorsing either.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the denial of the infinite not impossible?
A) Because the infinite does not exist.
B) Because lack of evidence for affirmation leads to denial, which is a form of non-existence, and non-existence is not impossible.
C) Because the infinite is not a possible entity.
D) Because the objection has been refuted.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between lack of evidence for negation and lack of evidence for affirmation is crucial to avoid faulty reasoning; lack of evidence for a positive claim does not prove its opposite unless supported by a decisive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold in your religious life for which you have no decisive evidence, only the absence of counterevidence. Are you willing to admit that your certainty might be misplaced? What decisive proof could you seek to ground that belief?
?
The objector assumed that lack of evidence for negation is equivalent to lack of evidence for affirmation. Where in your own reasoning about Islam—whether in matters of theology, history, or practice—have you unconsciously treated absence of evidence as evidence of absence? How can you break this habit starting today?
?
The author emphasizes that certainty about the absence of a prophet after Muhammad rests on decisive proof, not just lack of evidence. Can you name one other essential Islamic belief that you hold with similar certainty? What is the decisive proof for it? If you cannot name one, what does that reveal about the foundation of your faith?

Dawaran: Co-extension as Proof of Causality
Defines dawaran with the Mu'tazila's example of injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of dawaran (co-extension) used in kalam to establish causality, and see it applied through the Mu'tazilite example of injustice.

Core Idea
Dawaran: when a factor is present, the ruling is present; when absent, the ruling is absent.

In this passage, the author explains a key argumentative method known as dawaran (co-extension). This method is used to prove that a certain attribute or condition is the cause of a ruling. The Mu'tazila's example of injustice—defining it as harm without prior sin or later compensation—serves as a concrete illustration. As we read, we will see how the presence and absence of these conditions perfectly correlate with the presence and absence of ugliness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a general principle followed by an example.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the common factor and ruling
The principle states that a common factor (al-mushtarak) and the ruling (al-ḥukm) co-vary. In the example, what is the common factor? (Hint: it is the set of conditions: no prior sin, no later compensation.)
The ruling is ugliness (qubh).
3
Trace the co-extension
Follow the logic: when the conditions are present, ugliness is present; when any condition is removed, ugliness disappears. This co-extension in existence and non-existence is the core of dawaran.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the ugliness of injustice is caused by these conditions. Think about what this implies for proving causality through co-extension.
Dawaran is one method; later scholars added conditions like suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) to avoid false causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ذلك المشترك وجد الحكم وكلما عدم عدم وذلك مثل ما قالت المعتزلة : من الإضرار بلا جناية سابقة، ولا عوض لاحق قبيح في الشاهد ثم إذا تأملنا وجدنا أن الفعل إذا وقع على هذه الوجوه كلها كان قبيحاً، وإذا زال عنه شيء من هذه القيود زال قبحه، فقد دار القبح مع هذه الاعتبارات وجوداً، وعدماً فعلمنا أن قبح الظلم معلل بها
That the common factor [when] the ruling exists, and whenever it is absent, the ruling is absent. An example is what the Mu'tazila said: that inflicting harm without prior sin and without subsequent compensation is ugly (qabīḥ) in the witnessed realm. Then if we reflect, we find that when the act occurs with all these aspects, it is ugly; and when any of these conditions is removed, its ugliness is removed. So ugliness co-extends (dāra) with these considerations in existence and non-existence, and we know that the ugliness of injustice is caused by them.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the method of dawaran (co-extension) as a way to establish causality. It begins by stating the principle: when a common factor is present, the ruling is present, and when it is absent, the ruling is absent. The Mu'tazila example is then given: an act of harm with no prior sin and no subsequent compensation is considered ugly in human experience. Upon reflection, when the act includes all these conditions, it is ugly; removing any condition removes the ugliness. Thus ugliness co-extends with these considerations both in existence and non-existence, leading to the conclusion that the ugliness of injustice is caused by them. The passage presents this as a proof for causality through co-extension.

Key Terms
دوران
علية
قبح
شاهد
مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dawaran alone is sufficient for causality without considering suitability (ṣalūḥiyya).
Correction: Dawaran is a strong indicator, but later scholars argued that a common factor must also be 'suitable' (munāsib) to truly be the cause; otherwise, mere co-occurrence may be coincidental.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's example used to illustrate dawaran?
A) An act of harm with prior sin and later compensation
B) An act of harm without prior sin and without later compensation
C) An act of harm with only prior sin
D) An act of harm with only later compensation

Key Takeaway
Dawaran is a method to infer causality by co-extension, as shown by the Mu'tazila's analysis of injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila identified that injustice is ugly because it lacks prior sin and later compensation. When have you committed an act that you considered justified because you thought it harmed no one or had no consequences? How might the dawaran method expose the hidden conditions that make it truly ugly?
?
This lesson shows that the ugliness of an act can be tied to specific conditions. Think of a habit or decision you defend as 'not that bad.' Using the logic of dawaran, what would need to be absent for you to call it truly ugly? Are you willing to examine those conditions honestly?
?
If dawaran is a tool to uncover causes, what is the cause behind your own reluctance to apply moral reasoning to your actions? Are you avoiding the co-extension of your own behavior with its true ruling?

Divine Attributes: Contingent vs. Necessary and the Mu'tazilī's Argument
Compound analogy and Mu'tazilī's refutation on divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilī's argument that divine attributes of willing are contingent and caused, while attributes of knowing are necessary and uncaused, and why this distinction matters for avoiding multiplicity of eternals.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are either contingent (caused) or necessary (uncaused), preventing multiple eternal beings.

In this passage, the author presents a compound analogy used by the Mu'tazilī to argue that God's attribute of willing is caused by will, while the attribute of knowing is not. The reasoning hinges on whether an attribute is contingent or necessary for God. This lesson unpacks the logic, showing how the Mu'tazilī defends his position and how the argument attempts to preserve divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and the full English translation. Notice the structure: the Mu'tazilī sets up a conditional argument based on the contingency of willing.
The passage includes commentary that explains each part of the argument.
2
Trace the argument for willing being contingent
Focus on why the Mu'tazilī claims willing (murīdiyya) is a contingent attribute. The text argues that if willing were necessary, it would be eternal, which would require the object of will to exist eternally.
This step shows the consequence of necessity for attributes.
3
Examine the rule on necessary attributes
Understand why necessary attributes cannot have a cause distinct from God. The commentary explains that a necessary attribute cannot be caused by something else, otherwise there would be a pre-eternal cause other than God.
This prevents multiplicity of eternal beings (ta'addud al-qudamā').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا قياس مركب الأصل كما عرفته في التذكرة فللمعتزلي أن يقول : إنما حكمت بأن مريدية الله تعالى معللة بالإرادة لأن المريدية عندنا صفة جائزة له، والصفات الجائزة معللة والعالمية صفة واجبة له تعالى والواجب لا يعلل فإن صح ما قلت من أن المريدية صفة جائزة ظهر الفرق وإلا منعت كون المريدية معللة بالإرادة قوله : ( لأن المريدية عندنا صفة جائزة له (تعالى) إذ لو كانت واجبة له تعالى لكانت أزلية، فيلزم وجود المراد في الأزل. قوله : ( والصفات الجائزة معللة ) أي الأحوال الجائزة معللة بصفات مغايرة لذاته تعالى، إذ لا يلزم تعدد القدماء. قوله : ( واجبة له تعالى) فتكون ثابتة في الأزل . قوله : ( والواجب لا يعلل ) بأمر مغاير لذاته تعالى إذ لو علل لكان علته في الأزل، فيلزم قدم غیره تعالى فلا يرد ما توهم أن كونها واجبة لذاته لا ينافي التعليل لعدم كونها واجبة بالذات قوله : ( والعالمية صفة واجبة له تعالى والواجب لا يعلل سيرده في الإلهيات بأن وجوبها له تعالى بمعنى امتناع خلو الذات عنها لا يمنع استنادها إلى صفة أخرى واجبة أيضاً، والغرض ها هنا مجرد نقل كلامهم.
This is a compound source analogy as you know it from the Tadhkira. The Mu'tazilī may say: I only judged that the attribute of willing (murīdiyya) in God is caused by will (irāda) because, according to us, willing is a contingent attribute for Him, and contingent attributes are caused, while the attribute of knowing ('ālamiyya) is a necessary attribute for Him, and the necessary is not caused. If what I said—that willing is a contingent attribute—is correct, the difference appears; otherwise, I deny that willing is caused by will. He said: (Because willing is a contingent attribute for Him, exalted) for if it were necessary for Him, exalted, it would be eternal, and then the existence of the object of will would be necessary in pre-eternity. He said: (And contingent attributes are caused) meaning contingent states are caused by attributes distinct from His essence, since multiplicity of eternal beings is not necessary. He said: (Necessary for Him, exalted) so it would be established in pre-eternity. He said: (And the necessary is not caused) by something distinct from His essence, for if it were caused, its cause would be in pre-eternity, leading to the pre-eternity of something other than Him. Thus, the misconception that its being necessary in itself does not preclude causation is refuted because it is not necessary by essence. He said: (And the attribute of knowing is necessary for Him, exalted, and the necessary is not caused) will be refuted in Ilāhiyyāt by saying that its necessity for Him in the sense of the impossibility of the essence being free from it does not prevent its being based on another attribute that is also necessary. And the purpose here is merely to transmit their statements.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines and refutes a compound analogy (qiyās murakkab al-aṣl) used by an opponent. The author first states that the Mu'tazilī can respond by distinguishing between two types of divine attributes. The argument is that the Mu'tazilī judges that God's attribute of willing (murīdiyya) is caused by will (irāda) because willing is a contingent attribute (ṣifa jā'iza) and contingent attributes are caused, whereas the attribute of knowing ('ālamiyya) is necessary (wājiba) and thus cannot be caused. If the contingency of willing is accepted, the difference is clear; otherwise, the Mu'tazilī can simply deny that willing is caused by will. The commentary then explains each step: if willing were necessary, it would be eternal, implying the eternal existence of its object; contingent attributes require a cause distinct from the essence to avoid multiple eternal beings; and necessary attributes are not caused because that would entail a pre-eternal cause other than God. The commentary also notes that a potential objection—that something necessary in itself could still be caused—is refuted because it is not necessary by essence. Finally, the commentary acknowledges that another argument about the necessity of 'ālamiyya will be addressed later in the section on Ilāhiyyāt, but the current purpose is just to transmit the opponent's statements.

Key Terms
قياس مركب الأصل
المريدية
صفة جائزة
صفة واجبة
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary attribute (like knowing) can be caused as long as it is necessary by essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the attribute of willing is not necessary by essence; if it were, it would be eternal and cause eternal existence of its object. Necessary attributes for God (like knowing) cannot have a cause distinct from God because that would entail a pre-eternal cause other than God, leading to multiplicity of eternal beings.

Checkpoint
Why does the Mu'tazilī claim that God's attribute of willing (murīdiyya) is caused by will (irāda)?
A) Because willing is a necessary attribute.
B) Because willing is a contingent attribute.
C) Because will is the only cause.
D) Because all attributes are caused.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilī's argument distinguishes contingent from necessary attributes to show that only contingent attributes need a cause, preserving God's essence from causal dependency and avoiding multiplicity of eternals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attribute of willing is contingent and caused, do you truly believe that every event in your life happens by God's will and that He causes it for a reason? Where in your life are you acting as if things are random or without purpose? Repent from that thought and affirm His active will in your daily affairs.
?
The Mu'tazilī argument protects God's oneness by rejecting multiple eternal beings. Are there any false 'eternals' in your heart—such as pride, worldly ambitions, or fears—that you have elevated to a permanent status? Identify one and resolve today to see it as contingent, subject to God's will, and only He is eternal.
?
Knowing that God's knowledge is necessary and uncaused means His knowledge of you is perfect and unchanging. Does that comfort you, or does it trouble you? Confess to yourself an area where you struggle to believe that God's knowledge is fully just and merciful, and ask Him to deepen your trust in His eternal omniscience.

Definition of Certainty and the First Two Necessary Propositions
Defines yaqin and lists first two of seven necessary certain propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of certainty (yaqin) as belief conforming to reality and excluding alternatives, and the first two types of necessary certain propositions: self-evident truths and propositions whose syllogisms are implicit.

Core Idea
Certainty is not just belief—it must match reality and be exclusive of alternatives.

The author begins by defining what it means to be certain (yaqin) in a logical sense, which is more than merely holding an opinion. Certainty requires both objective correspondence and subjective conviction that the matter cannot be otherwise. This foundational definition sets the stage for enumerating the seven types of necessary propositions that serve as the starting points of all rational inquiry. In this lesson, we examine the first two types: self-evident truths and propositions that carry their own proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of yaqin
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where yaqin is defined. Identify the two conditions: conformity to reality and belief that it cannot be otherwise.
The word 'i'tiqad' means belief or conviction.
2
Understand why the second condition is necessary
Consider why belief that it cannot be otherwise is needed. This distinguishes certainty from mere opinion or conjecture.
Without this, one could have a true belief but still doubt.
3
Identify the first type: al-awwaliyyat
Find the description of self-evident propositions. Note the condition: the soul cannot be empty of them after conceiving the terms and the relation.
Some are clear to all, others only to penetrating minds.
4
Study the second type: qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha
Read the explanation and the example of 'four is even'. Understand that the syllogism is implicit in the conception of the terms.
These are close to self-evident but require a small inference.
5
Compare the two types
Think about how these two types differ: one is immediately known, the other requires a quick syllogism that is inseparable from the terms.
Both are necessary and certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقطعية ( أي اليقينية واليقين هو اعتقاد أن الشيء كذا مع مطابقته للواقع واعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون إلا كذا والمراد أن القطعية الضرورية التي هي المبادي الأول ( سبع: الأولى (الأوليات ) وهي ( ما لا تخلو النفس عنها بعد تصور الطرفين) وملاحظة النسبة بينهما فمنها ما هو جلي عند الكل لوضوح تصورات ،أطرافه، ومنها ما هو خفي لخفاء في تصوراته، وهذا القسم لا يخفى أيضاً على الأذهان المشتعلة النافذة في التصورات ( الثانية قضايا قياساتها معها وهي قضايا تكون تصورات أطرافها ملزومة لقياس يوجب الحكم بينه، أو هي قريبة من الأوليات نحو الأربعة منقسمة بمتساويين فهي زوج) فالقضية هي قولنا الأربعة زوج، والقياس اللازم لتصوراتها قولنا هي منقسمة
So the certain ones (i.e., the certain/yakin ones) and certainty is a belief that a thing is such, with its conformity to reality, and a belief that it cannot be except such, and what is intended is that the necessary certain propositions which are the first principles are seven: first: the self-evident (al-awwaliyyat) which are what the soul is not empty of after conceiving the two extremes and observing the relation between them. Among them is what is clear to everyone due to the clarity of the conceptions of its terms, and among them is what is hidden due to obscurity in its conceptions, and this type is not hidden from minds that are keen and penetrating in conceptions. Second: propositions whose syllogisms are with them, which are propositions such that the conceptions of their extremes imply a syllogism that necessitates the judgment between them, or they are close to the self-evident, like 'four is divisible into two equals, so it is even'. So the proposition is our statement 'four is even', and the syllogism necessitated by its conceptions is our statement 'it is divisible'.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines certainty (yaqin) and begins enumerating the seven necessary certain propositions (al-qat'iyya al-daruriyya) that serve as first principles (al-mabadi' al-uwal). The author first defines yaqin as belief (i'tiqad) that a thing is such-and-such, with the condition that this belief corresponds to reality (mutabaqa li-l-waqi'), and further that one believes it cannot be otherwise (la yumkin an yakuna illa kadha). This second condition excludes probable opinion (zann) and blind acceptance (taqlid). The definition emphasizes both objective truth and subjective certainty. Then the author states that the necessary certain propositions are seven, and proceeds to explain the first two. The first type is al-awwaliyyat (self-evident propositions), which are judgments that the mind cannot withhold assent from once it conceives the subject and predicate and observes the relation between them. Some are clear to all due to the clarity of the concepts involved; others are less clear to some because of obscurity in the concepts. The second type is 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them' (qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha). These are propositions where simply conceiving the terms implies a syllogism that yields the judgment, so they are close to self-evident. The example is 'four is even': the conception of 'four' implies divisibility into two equals, which leads to the judgment of evenness. The proposition is 'four is even', and the implicit syllogism is 'four is divisible into two equals, and whatever is divisible into two equals is even, so four is even.' This passage establishes the foundational categories of necessary knowledge in Islamic logic and theology.

Key Terms
قطعية
يقين
اعتقاد
مطابقة
ضرورية
مبادئ أول
أوليات
قياساتها معها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all certain knowledge is necessary (i.e., self-evident or immediately known).
Correction: The passage specifies that only a subset of certain knowledge is necessary and then enumerates seven types; certainty itself is broader.

Checkpoint
What are the two conditions for certainty (yaqin) according to this passage?
A) Belief and action
B) Correspondence to reality and belief that it cannot be otherwise
C) Sense perception and rational deduction
D) Divine revelation and consensus

Key Takeaway
Certainty requires both that a belief matches reality and that one holds it as necessary, and the first two types of necessary propositions are self-evident truths and those with implicit syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold with absolute certainty. Does it satisfy both conditions from this passage: conformity to reality AND the impossibility of being otherwise? If not, are you truly certain, or merely confident? What would it take for you to honestly admit it might be wrong?
?
This lesson teaches that some certainties require effort to see (the hidden awwaliyyat). Is there an area in your life where you have stopped seeking clarity, content with comfortable assumptions? Will you commit to examining one such area today with the piercing honesty this definition demands?
?
You claim to believe in Allah and the Hereafter with yaqin. But does your daily life reflect the 'cannot be otherwise' condition? If your certainty were truly necessary, how would your priorities, fears, and habits change? What would be different tomorrow morning if you lived as if this certainty were impossible to deny?

First Principles (Awwaliyat): Self-Evident Propositions
Commentator defines awwaliyat and divides them into clear and hidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what awwaliyat are, their defining condition, and the distinction between clear and hidden first principles.

Core Idea
First principles are self-evident truths that the mind cannot deny once it understands the terms.

This lesson introduces the first category of certain premises in Islamic logic: awwaliyat, or first principles. These are foundational propositions that the mind immediately accepts once it grasps the subject, predicate, and their relation. The commentator explains that not all first principles are equally obvious; some are clear to everyone while others require a sharper intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of awwaliyat and the two types mentioned.
2
Understand the definition
Focus on the condition: 'after conceptualizing the two extremes and noting the relation between them.' Reflect on why this condition is necessary.
3
Grasp the division
Distinguish between clear (jali) and hidden (khafi) awwaliyat. Consider the exceptions: who qualifies as 'everyone' and what 'obscurity' means.
The commentator clarifies that 'everyone' means those with the capacity for perception, excluding children, the insane, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى (الأوليات ) وهي ( ما لا تخلو النفس عنها بعد تصور الطرفين) وملاحظة النسبة بينهما فمنها ما هو جلي عند الكل لوضوح تصورات أطرافه، ومنها ما هو خفي لخفاء في تصوراته، وهذا القسم لا يخفى أيضاً على الأذهان المشتعلة النافذة في التصورات. قوله : ( عند الكل أي كل من له استعداد الإدراك، فلا يرد الصبيان والمجانين، وصاحب البلادة المتناهية والمدنس بالاعتقادات الباطلة المنكر للبديهيات . قوله : ( لخفاء في تصوراته إما لعدم الوضوح أو لكونها نظرية .
The first: al-awwaliyat (first principles), and they are what the soul is not devoid of after conceptualizing the two extremes and noting the relation between them. Among them is what is evident to everyone due to the clarity of the concepts of its extremes, and among them is what is hidden due to obscurity in its concepts. And this section is not hidden from minds that are burning and penetrating into concepts. His saying: 'to everyone' i.e., everyone who has the capacity for perception, so children, the insane, the one with extreme dullness, and the one defiled by false beliefs who denies self-evident truths are not included. His saying: 'due to obscurity in its concepts' either due to lack of clarity or because they are theoretical.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the first type of certain premises: al-awwaliyat (first principles). The main text states that awwaliyat are propositions from which the soul cannot dissociate after conceptualizing the two terms (subject and predicate) and noticing the relation between them. The commentator then divides awwaliyat into two kinds: those that are evident to everyone (jaliyyun) because the concepts of their terms are clear, and those that are hidden (khafiyyun) due to obscurity in their concepts. However, even the hidden ones are not hidden from minds that are 'burning and penetrating'—that is, sharp and deep minds. The commentator then explains the phrase 'to everyone' (عند الكل): it means everyone who has the capacity for perception (isti`dad al-idrak). Therefore, children, the insane, the extremely dull, and those polluted by false beliefs who deny self-evident truths are excluded. He also explains 'due to obscurity in its concepts' as either because the concepts themselves are not clear or because they are theoretical (nazariyya), meaning they require some reflection.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Awwaliyat are self-evident to every person without exception.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'everyone' refers to those with the capacity for perception, excluding children, the insane, the extremely dull, and those defiled by false beliefs.

Checkpoint

A) All human beings without exception
B) Everyone who has the capacity for perception
C) Only scholars
D) Only those who accept the truth

Key Takeaway
First principles (awwaliyat) are self-evident propositions that the mind immediately accepts after understanding the terms, and they are divided into clear (jali) and hidden (khafi).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you consider self-evident. Are you absolutely certain it is true, or could it be that you have not fully examined its terms? This lesson challenges you to question the clarity of your own first principles.
?
Have you ever denied a truth because your mind was clouded by false beliefs or assumptions? Identify one instance and confront it: what would it take for you to accept that truth now?
?
The hidden awwaliyat (khafi) require a 'burning and penetrating' mind to grasp. Is your mind sharp and eager to uncover truths, or do you settle for superficial understanding? Commit to one way you will deepen your conceptual clarity today.

Propositions with Their Syllogisms (Qadaya Qiyasatuha Ma'aha)
Definition of qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha with example and explanatory commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what propositions with their syllogisms are, how they differ from first principles, and the role of the implicit syllogism in their certainty.

Core Idea
These propositions carry their own proof, requiring only a quick syllogism for certainty.

Having covered the first type of certain premises (first principles), the commentator now introduces the second type: propositions whose syllogisms are inherent to them. These are judgments that, once you understand the terms, a simple syllogism immediately comes to mind, making them nearly as certain as first principles. In this lesson, we will break down the definition, examine the classic example of 'four is even', and clarify the subtle difference from immediate knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow along with the literal translation. Notice the compact style of the matn and the explanatory comments.
The passage includes both the main text and the commentator's gloss.
2
Understand the definition and example
Focus on the definition: propositions whose conceptualizations necessitate a syllogism that yields the judgment. Then examine the example: 'four is even' with the syllogism 'four is divisible into two equal halves; everything divisible into two equal halves is even'.
The example shows two parts: the proposition itself and the implicit syllogism.
3
Distinguish from first principles
Notice the commentator's comparison: these are 'close to first principles' because conceptualizing the terms suffices for certainty, but unlike first principles, they require a mediation (wasta) — the syllogism. Internalize this distinction.
4
Reflect on the commentator's note
Read the final part where the commentator discusses the phrasing. Understand why the author presented the syllogism before the proposition, and the alternative formulation he suggests.
This helps in grasping the logic of presentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية قضايا قياساتها معها وهي قضايا تكون تصورات أطرافها ملزومة لقياس يوجب الحكم بينه، أو هي قريبة من الأوليات نحو الأربعة منقسمة بمتساويين فهي زوج) فالقضية هي قولنا الأربعة زوج، والقياس اللازم لتصوراتها قولنا هي منقسمة بمتساويين، وكل منقسم بمتساويين زوج. قوله : قريبة من الأوليات لأن تصور الطرفين كاف في الجزم فيها ، إلا أن في الأوليات بلا واسطة وفي القضايا المذكورة بالواسطة . قوله: (فالقضية إلخ) إشارة إلى أن قوله نحو إلخ مثال القياس والقضية معاً، قدم مثال القياس لكونه أصلاً لها، وإن كان الظاهر أن يقول : نحو الأربعة زوج لأنها منقسمة بمتساويين.
The second: propositions whose syllogisms are with them, and they are propositions whose conceptualizations of their extremes are necessarily entailing a syllogism that necessitates the judgment between them, or they are close to first principles, for example: four is divisible into two equal halves, so it is even. So the proposition is our statement 'four is even', and the syllogism necessarily entailed by their conceptualizations is our statement: 'it is divisible into two equal halves, and everything divisible into two equal halves is even.' His saying: 'close to first principles' because conceptualizing the two extremes suffices for certainty in them, except that in first principles it is without mediation, and in the mentioned propositions it is with mediation. His saying: 'so the proposition etc.' is an indication that his saying 'for example' etc. is an example of both the syllogism and the proposition together. He presented the example of the syllogism because it is the basis for it, although the apparent way is to say: 'for example, four is even because it is divisible into two equal halves.'
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of certain premises: qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha (propositions whose syllogisms are with them). The main text states that these are propositions where understanding the two terms automatically entails a syllogism that yields the judgment. They are close to awwaliyat (first principles). The example given is 'four is divisible into two equal halves, so it is even.' The proposition is 'four is even', and the implicit syllogism is: 'four is divisible into two equal halves; everything divisible into two equal halves is even; therefore four is even.' The commentator explains that these are 'close to first principles' because conceptualizing the two terms is enough for certainty, but unlike awwaliyat, they require an intermediate step (the syllogism). In awwaliyat, the judgment is immediate; here, it is mediated by a syllogism that is quickly grasped. The commentator also notes that the main text's phrasing presents the example in a way that illustrates both the proposition and the syllogism, and he suggests an alternative formulation.

القياس

D) They are not known by everyone

Key Takeaway
Propositions with their syllogisms are certain premises that require a quickly grasped syllogism, distinguishing them from immediate first principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief as certain, do you ever pause to examine whether your certainty is immediate (like 'two is greater than one') or mediated by a quick argument you've internalized? Which type of certainty is more common in your daily judgments?
?
The lesson says conceptualizing the extremes suffices for certainty, but with a small step. Is there an area of your faith where you rely on a 'quick syllogism' (e.g., 'God is just, therefore suffering has a purpose') without fully articulating it? Will you take time to honestly trace that reasoning?
?
If someone challenges a belief you consider 'obvious,' would you be able to produce the implicit syllogism that grounds it? If not, is your certainty truly justified, or is it merely habit?

Mushahadat: Observations and Their Classification
Commentator defines mushahadat and distinguishes external and internal sense, introducing wijdaniyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mushahadat (observations) and their division into judgments based on external and internal senses.

Core Idea
Observations are judgments by the intellect based on sense alone.

In this passage, the commentator lays out the third type of certain premises: observations (mushahadat). He distinguishes between external and internal senses and explains what the key terms 'mushahadat' and 'wijdaniyyat' mean. Let's explore how these judgments are formed and categorized.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mushahadat and examples
Start by reading the Arabic definition of mushahadat and the examples given for external sense (sun exists, fire hot) and internal sense (having thoughts, fear).
Notice that both types are introduced with 'by mere sense' (بمجرد الحس).
2
Identify the two types of mushahadat
The passage clearly divides mushahadat into two: based on external sense (al-hiss al-zahir) and internal sense (al-hiss al-batin). Under internal sense, there is a subdivision into wijdaniyyat (inner feelings) and things known by internal sense without organs (like self-awareness).
3
Understand the relationship between mushahadat and wijdaniyyat
The commentator explains that mushahadat and wijdaniyyat overlap partially: external sensations are mushahadat but not wijdaniyyat; self-awareness is wijdaniyyat but not mushahadat; they intersect in what is known by internal sense (like emotions).
This is a key logical relationship: general-special from one aspect (عموم وخصوص من وجه).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة المشاهدات وهي ( ما يحكم به العقل بمجرد الحس) الظاهر مثل حكمنا بوجود الشمس وكونها مضيئة، وكون النار حارة وتسمى هذه محسوسات أو الحس الباطن كالحكم بأن لنا فكرة وأن لنا خوفاً وغضباً، وتسمى هذه وجدانية وقضايا اعتقادية، ويعد منها ما نجده بنفوسنا لا بآلاتها كشعورنا بذواتنا وبأفعال ذواتنا. واعلم أن الحس لا يفيد إلا حكماً جزئياً كما في قولك هذه النار حارة، وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة فمستفاد من الإحساس بجزئيات كثيرة مع. قوله : ( بمجرد الحس) أي بدون التكرار والحدس وأخبار جماعة . قوله : (أو) الحس الباطن اختلف في أن هذه القوة ماذا أهي إحدى القوى المدركة المشهورة أم لا ، قال الإمام كلا القولين محتمل ثم إذا كانت إحداهما، فالظاهر أنها الوهم، فالمعاني الجزئية الجسمانية التي إدراكها بحصولها نفسها تسمى وجدانيات، والتي إدراكها بمثالها وهميات كذا حققه بعض الناظرين في حواشي شرح مختصر الأصول العضدي. قوله : ( ويعد منها إلخ) يعني أن بين الوجدانيات والمشاهدات عموماً وخصوصاً من وجه فإن المحسوسات مشاهدات ولیست بوجدانيات وما نجده بنفوسنا وجدانيات وليست بمشاهدات ويجتمعان فيما تعلمه بالحس الباطن . قوله : ( واعلم إلخ) المقصود تحقيق أن الحسيات هي القضايا الجزئية دون القضايا الكلية المترتبة عليها، وبيان مدخلية العقل في تلك القضايا الجزئية في الإنسان . قوله : (لا يفيد إلا حكماً جزئياً ) إذ لا سبيل له إلا إلى الإدراك الجزئي كهذه النار في وقت جزئي، فالحسيات كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة بمشاهدة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع، كذا في شرح حكمة الإشراق . قوله: (فمستفاد إلخ) أي استفادة العقل إذا وقع له الإحساس بثبوت المحمول لجزئيات كثيرة من الموضوع كذا في المحاكمات فهو حكم أولي موقوف على تكرر الإحساس مع الوقوف على العلة وبهذا يمتاز عن المجربات فإنه لا وقوف فيها على العلة، وإن كان يشاركها قوله وتسمى هذه وجدانية إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأن الوجدانيات لا تختص بالعقلاء، بل توجد في البهائم أيضا إذ إدراك الجوع والألم والعطش، مما لا نزاع في حصوله لها، فلا معنى الوجدانيات من المشاهدات ثم تفسيرها بأنها قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحس، الظاهر أو الباطن اللهم، إلا أن يقال المراد إدراك حصولها، وهذا غير حاصل للبهائم، ويمكن أن يقال : بعد تسليم إطلاق الوجدانيات على الحاصل للبهائم المعدود من المشاهدات بعض الوجدانيات فبينهما عموم من وجه وإنما قال : ويعد منها ما تجده بنفوسنا إذ لا دخل للحس فيه إلا أنه عد منها تغليباً. قوله : ( وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة إلخ) وقد يقال : هذه القضية الكلية من المجربات لصدق تعريفها عليها .
The third: al-mushahadat (observations), and they are what the intellect judges by mere sense: external sense, like our judgment that the sun exists and is luminous, and that fire is hot; these are called mahsusat (sensible things). Or internal sense, like the judgment that we have thought, that we have fear and anger; these are called wijdaniyyat (inner feelings) and i`tiqadiyya (beliefs). And from them is counted what we find in ourselves not by its organs, like our awareness of ourselves and of our own acts. Know that sense does not yield except a particular judgment, as in your statement 'this fire is hot'. As for the judgment that 'every fire is hot', it is derived from sensation of many particulars with... His saying: 'by mere sense' i.e., without repetition, intuition, or reports of a group. His saying: 'or' internal sense: there is disagreement about what this faculty is; is it one of the well-known perceiving faculties or not? Al-Imam said both views are possible. Then if it is one of them, apparently it is wahm (estimation). So the particular corporeal meanings whose perception is by their very occurrence are called wijdaniyyat, and those whose perception is by their likeness are called wahmiyyat. Thus verified some of the investigators in the glosses on Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul al-`Adudi. His saying: 'and from them is counted etc.' means that between wijdaniyyat and mushahadat there is a general-special relationship from one aspect: for mahsusat are mushahadat but not wijdaniyyat, and what we find in ourselves are wijdaniyyat but not mushahadat, and they combine in what is known by internal sense. His saying: 'and know etc.' The purpose is to verify that sensibles (hissiyyat) are particular judgments, not the universal judgments consequent upon them, and to clarify the involvement of the intellect in those particular judgments in humans. His saying: 'does not yield except a particular judgment' because it has no way except to particular perception, like this fire at a particular time. So all sensibles are particular judgments obtained by observing the relation of the predicate to the subject, as in Sharh Hikmat al-Ishraq. His saying: 'it is derived etc.' i.e., the intellect's derivation, when it has sensation of the predicate's being established for many particulars of the subject, as in al-Muhakamat; it is a primary judgment dependent on repetition of sensation along with knowledge of the cause, and by this it is distinguished from tajribiyyat (experiential judgments), for in them there is no knowledge of the cause, although it shares with them. His saying: 'and these are called wijdaniyya etc.' An objection was raised against it: Wijdaniyyat are not peculiar to rational beings, but also exist in beasts, for the perception of hunger, pain, and thirst is indisputably possessed by them, so there is no meaning to counting wijdaniyyat among mushahadat, then interpreting them as propositions judged by the intellect via sense, external or internal, unless it is said that what is meant is perception of their occurrence, and this is not attained by beasts. It can be said: after acknowledging the application of wijdaniyyat to what occurs in beasts, the counted among mushahadat are some wijdaniyyat, so between them is a general-special relationship from one aspect. He only said 'and from them is counted what we find in ourselves' because sense has no part in it, except that it was counted among them by predominance. His saying: 'and as for the judgment that every fire is hot etc.' And it may be said: this universal proposition is from mujarribat (experiential judgments) because its definition applies to it.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of certain premises: al-mushahadat (observations). The main text says they are what the intellect judges by mere sense, and divides sense into external (al-zahir) like judging that fire is hot, and internal (al-batin) like judging that we have thoughts or feelings. The latter are called wijdaniyyat (inner feelings) and include self-awareness. The commentator explains 'by mere sense' as without repetition, intuition (hads), or reports (akhbar). He then discusses internal sense, noting disagreement whether it is one of the known faculties; if it is, likely it is wahm (estimation). He also clarifies that wijdaniyyat and mushahadat overlap partially: external sensations are mushahadat but not wijdaniyyat; self-awareness is wijdaniyyat but not mushahadat; they intersect in what is known by internal sense (like emotions). The commentator then emphasizes a key point: sense only gives particular judgments (e.g., 'this fire is hot'). The universal judgment 'every fire is hot' comes from repeated sensation plus reason, and this is a primary judgment dependent on repetition and knowledge of the cause ('illa). This distinguishes it from experimental judgments (tajribiyyat) where the cause is not known. An objection is raised: wijdaniyyat occur in beasts too, so they should not be counted as mushahadat (which require intellect). The commentator offers two replies: either wijdaniyyat in beasts are not a perception of occurrence (idrak husuliha) as in humans, or they are counted under mushahadat by predominance (taghliban). Finally, a note suggests that the universal judgment 'every fire is hot' might be classified as mujarribat (experiential) instead.

Key Terms
المشاهدات
الحس الظاهر
الحس الباطن
الوجدانيات

Key Takeaway
Mushahadat are judgments of the intellect based solely on sense, divided into external and internal types, with a partial overlap between mushahadat and wijdaniyyat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson teaches that both are valid. What inner truth have you been ignoring because you thought it wasn't 'observable'?
?
The passage says sense alone gives only particular judgments. When you say 'I know Allah exists', is that a particular or universal judgment? How do you move from the particular signs you sense to the universal truth of His existence? This lesson challenges you to examine the basis of your certainty.
?
