From Particular to Universal: The Role of Reason and the Objection
Commentator explains that sense yields only particular judgments, how universals are derived, addresses an objection about wijdaniyyat in beasts, and distinguishes from tajribiyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sense yields only particular judgments, how universal judgments are derived through repetition and knowledge of the cause, and the objection that wijdaniyyat exist in beasts.

Core Idea
Sense yields only particular judgments; universal judgments require reason and repetition.

In this part of the passage, the commentator makes a crucial epistemological point: sense alone cannot provide universal judgments. He explains the process by which we arrive at universals like 'every fire is hot' through repeated sensation and knowledge of the cause. He then addresses an objection regarding wijdaniyyat in beasts and clarifies the distinction from experimental judgments (tajribiyyat).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on particular and universal judgments
Start by reading the Arabic from 'واعلم أن الحس لا يفيد إلا حكماً جزئياً' to the end. Notice the key claim: sense only gives particular judgments.
2
Understand the derivation of universal judgments
The commentator explains that the universal judgment (e.g., 'every fire is hot') is derived from sensation of many particulars along with knowledge of the cause (al-`illa). This process distinguishes it from mere repetition.
This is a primary judgment (ḥukm awwalī) dependent on repetition and cause.
3
Examine the objection about wijdaniyyat in beasts
An objection is raised: wijdaniyyat (like hunger, pain) also occur in beasts, so how can they be counted among mushahadat, which involve intellect? The commentator offers two responses: either beasts lack perception of occurrence (idrāk ḥuṣūlihā), or wijdaniyyat are counted among mushahadat by predominance (taghlīban).
This highlights the role of intellect in mushahadat.
4
Note the distinction from tajribiyyat
Finally, a note suggests that the universal 'every fire is hot' might be classified as mujarribat (experiential) because its definition applies. This shows the nuance in classifying premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة المشاهدات وهي ( ما يحكم به العقل بمجرد الحس) الظاهر مثل حكمنا بوجود الشمس وكونها مضيئة، وكون النار حارة وتسمى هذه محسوسات أو الحس الباطن كالحكم بأن لنا فكرة وأن لنا خوفاً وغضباً، وتسمى هذه وجدانية وقضايا اعتقادية، ويعد منها ما نجده بنفوسنا لا بآلاتها كشعورنا بذواتنا وبأفعال ذواتنا. واعلم أن الحس لا يفيد إلا حكماً جزئياً كما في قولك هذه النار حارة، وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة فمستفاد من الإحساس بجزئيات كثيرة مع. قوله : ( بمجرد الحس) أي بدون التكرار والحدس وأخبار جماعة . قوله : (أو) الحس الباطن اختلف في أن هذه القوة ماذا أهي إحدى القوى المدركة المشهورة أم لا ، قال الإمام كلا القولين محتمل ثم إذا كانت إحداهما، فالظاهر أنها الوهم، فالمعاني الجزئية الجسمانية التي إدراكها بحصولها نفسها تسمى وجدانيات، والتي إدراكها بمثالها وهميات كذا حققه بعض الناظرين في حواشي شرح مختصر الأصول العضدي. قوله : ( ويعد منها إلخ) يعني أن بين الوجدانيات والمشاهدات عموماً وخصوصاً من وجه فإن المحسوسات مشاهدات ولیست بوجدانيات وما نجده بنفوسنا وجدانيات وليست بمشاهدات ويجتمعان فيما تعلمه بالحس الباطن . قوله : ( واعلم إلخ) المقصود تحقيق أن الحسيات هي القضايا الجزئية دون القضايا الكلية المترتبة عليها، وبيان مدخلية العقل في تلك القضايا الجزئية في الإنسان . قوله : (لا يفيد إلا حكماً جزئياً ) إذ لا سبيل له إلا إلى الإدراك الجزئي كهذه النار في وقت جزئي، فالحسيات كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة بمشاهدة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع، كذا في شرح حكمة الإشراق . قوله: (فمستفاد إلخ) أي استفادة العقل إذا وقع له الإحساس بثبوت المحمول لجزئيات كثيرة من الموضوع كذا في المحاكمات فهو حكم أولي موقوف على تكرر الإحساس مع الوقوف على العلة وبهذا يمتاز عن المجربات فإنه لا وقوف فيها على العلة، وإن كان يشاركها قوله وتسمى هذه وجدانية إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأن الوجدانيات لا تختص بالعقلاء، بل توجد في البهائم أيضا إذ إدراك الجوع والألم والعطش، مما لا نزاع في حصوله لها، فلا معنى الوجدانيات من المشاهدات ثم تفسيرها بأنها قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحس، الظاهر أو الباطن اللهم، إلا أن يقال المراد إدراك حصولها، وهذا غير حاصل للبهائم، ويمكن أن يقال : بعد تسليم إطلاق الوجدانيات على الحاصل للبهائم المعدود من المشاهدات بعض الوجدانيات فبينهما عموم من وجه وإنما قال : ويعد منها ما تجده بنفوسنا إذ لا دخل للحس فيه إلا أنه عد منها تغليباً. قوله : ( وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة إلخ) وقد يقال : هذه القضية الكلية من المجربات لصدق تعريفها عليها .
The third: al-mushahadat (observations), and they are what the intellect judges by mere sense: external sense, like our judgment that the sun exists and is luminous, and that fire is hot; these are called mahsusat (sensible things). Or internal sense, like the judgment that we have thought, that we have fear and anger; these are called wijdaniyyat (inner feelings) and i`tiqadiyya (beliefs). And from them is counted what we find in ourselves not by its organs, like our awareness of ourselves and of our own acts. Know that sense does not yield except a particular judgment, as in your statement 'this fire is hot'. As for the judgment that 'every fire is hot', it is derived from sensation of many particulars with... His saying: 'by mere sense' i.e., without repetition, intuition, or reports of a group. His saying: 'or' internal sense: there is disagreement about what this faculty is; is it one of the well-known perceiving faculties or not? Al-Imam said both views are possible. Then if it is one of them, apparently it is wahm (estimation). So the particular corporeal meanings whose perception is by their very occurrence are called wijdaniyyat, and those whose perception is by their likeness are called wahmiyyat. Thus verified some of the investigators in the glosses on Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul al-`Adudi. His saying: 'and from them is counted etc.' means that between wijdaniyyat and mushahadat there is a general-special relationship from one aspect: for mahsusat are mushahadat but not wijdaniyyat, and what we find in ourselves are wijdaniyyat but not mushahadat, and they combine in what is known by internal sense. His saying: 'and know etc.' The purpose is to verify that sensibles (hissiyyat) are particular judgments, not the universal judgments consequent upon them, and to clarify the involvement of the intellect in those particular judgments in humans. His saying: 'does not yield except a particular judgment' because it has no way except to particular perception, like this fire at a particular time. So all sensibles are particular judgments obtained by observing the relation of the predicate to the subject, as in Sharh Hikmat al-Ishraq. His saying: 'it is derived etc.' i.e., the intellect's derivation, when it has sensation of the predicate's being established for many particulars of the subject, as in al-Muhakamat; it is a primary judgment dependent on repetition of sensation along with knowledge of the cause, and by this it is distinguished from tajribiyyat (experiential judgments), for in them there is no knowledge of the cause, although it shares with them. His saying: 'and these are called wijdaniyya etc.' An objection was raised against it: Wijdaniyyat are not peculiar to rational beings, but also exist in beasts, for the perception of hunger, pain, and thirst is indisputably possessed by them, so there is no meaning to counting wijdaniyyat among mushahadat, then interpreting them as propositions judged by the intellect via sense, external or internal, unless it is said that what is meant is perception of their occurrence, and this is not attained by beasts. It can be said: after acknowledging the application of wijdaniyyat to what occurs in beasts, the counted among mushahadat are some wijdaniyyat, so between them is a general-special relationship from one aspect. He only said 'and from them is counted what we find in ourselves' because sense has no part in it, except that it was counted among them by predominance. His saying: 'and as for the judgment that every fire is hot etc.' And it may be said: this universal proposition is from mujarribat (experiential judgments) because its definition applies to it.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of certain premises: al-mushahadat (observations). The main text says they are what the intellect judges by mere sense, and divides sense into external (al-zahir) like judging that fire is hot, and internal (al-batin) like judging that we have thoughts or feelings. The latter are called wijdaniyyat (inner feelings) and include self-awareness. The commentator explains 'by mere sense' as without repetition, intuition (hads), or reports (akhbar). He then discusses internal sense, noting disagreement whether it is one of the known faculties; if it is, likely it is wahm (estimation). He also clarifies that wijdaniyyat and mushahadat overlap partially: external sensations are mushahadat but not wijdaniyyat; self-awareness is wijdaniyyat but not mushahadat; they intersect in what is known by internal sense (like emotions). The commentator then emphasizes a key point: sense only gives particular judgments (e.g., 'this fire is hot'). The universal judgment 'every fire is hot' comes from repeated sensation plus reason, and this is a primary judgment dependent on repetition and knowledge of the cause ('illa). This distinguishes it from experimental judgments (tajribiyyat) where the cause is not known. An objection is raised: wijdaniyyat occur in beasts too, so they should not be counted as mushahadat (which require intellect). The commentator offers two replies: either wijdaniyyat in beasts are not a perception of occurrence (idrak husuliha) as in humans, or they are counted under mushahadat by predominance (taghliban). Finally, a note suggests that the universal judgment 'every fire is hot' might be classified as mujarribat (experiential) instead.

Key Terms
الحكم الجزئي
الحكم الكلي
الوجدانيات
المجربات
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sensory observations directly give universal statements (e.g., 'fire is hot' as a universal rule) without any additional reasoning.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that sense only provides particular judgments; universal judgments require repeated observation and knowledge of the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is required to derive the universal judgment 'every fire is hot' from sensory observations?
A) A single observation
B) Repeated sensation alone
C) Repeated sensation along with knowledge of the cause
D) A syllogism from first principles

Key Takeaway
Sense gives only particular judgments; universal judgments are formed by repeated observation and understanding of the cause, and the classification of premises like wijdaniyyat requires careful handling of objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel hunger or pain, do you simply experience it, or do you also reflect on the fact that you are experiencing it? How does this distinction between mere sensation and reflective awareness affect your certainty about what is real?
?
Consider a belief you hold about the world that is based on repeated experience (e.g., 'water always quenches thirst'). Have you ever genuinely examined whether you know the cause behind it, or do you simply rely on repetition? What would it mean for your faith if you discovered the cause was different?
?
The commentator argues that a universal judgment like 'every fire is hot' requires both repetition and knowledge of the cause. In your journey of belief, are there universal claims about Allah or the afterlife that you accept merely from repetition (tradition) rather than from understanding the cause? What would it take for you to truly 'know' them — not just assume them?

Observational Premises: Definition and Types
Defines observational premises and distinguishes external from internal sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of observational premises and the distinction between judgments based on external senses and those based on internal senses.

Core Idea
Observational premises are what the intellect judges purely through sense.

This lesson establishes the foundational concept of observational premises (al-mushāhadāt). The author defines them as judgments made by the intellect through mere sense perception, without additional reasoning. He then clarifies that sense includes both external senses, such as sight and touch, and internal senses, such as awareness of one's own thoughts and feelings. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's precise wording: 'what the intellect judges by mere sense'.
The definition excludes judgments involving reasoning.
2
Identify the two types of sense
The passage explicitly divides sense into external and internal. Find the examples given for each type and understand how they differ.
3
Note the addition of self-awareness
The author adds that self-awareness and awareness of one's own actions are also counted among observational premises, even though they are not obtained by bodily sense organs.
This is an important nuance for internal sense.

وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة إلخ) وقد يقال : هذه القضية الكلية من المجربات لصدق تعريفها عليها .
The sixth observation – the sixth objective: on premises. ... (third: observational premises) which are (what the intellect judges by mere sense) – the external, like our judgment that the sun exists and that it is luminous, and that fire is hot; these are called sensibles. Or the internal sense, like the judgment that we have thought and that we have fear and anger; these are called found (wijdāniyya) and credal propositions. And among them is counted what we find in our souls not by their instruments, like our awareness of ourselves and of the acts of our selves. Know that sense does not yield except a particular judgment, as in your saying 'this fire is hot'. As for the judgment that every fire is hot, it is derived from sensation of many particulars along with [reason]. His saying: 'by mere sense' i.e. without repetition, intuition, or reports of a group. His saying: 'or the internal sense' – there is disagreement about what this faculty is: is it one of the well-known perceiving faculties or not? The Imam said both views are possible. Then if it is one of them, it appears that it is the estimation (wahm). So the particular corporeal meanings whose perception is by their very occurrence are called found (wijdāniyyāt), and those whose perception is by their likeness are estimative (wahmiyyāt) – thus verified by some researchers in the glosses of the commentary on Mukhtaṣar al-Uṣūl al-‘Aḍudī. His saying: 'and among them is counted etc.' means that between the found (wijdāniyyāt) and the observational there is a general and specific relation in some respect: for the sensibles are observational but not found, and what we find in our souls are found but not observational, and they intersect in what is known by internal sense. His saying: 'Know etc.' The purpose is to verify that sense-based propositions are particular propositions, not the universal propositions that follow from them, and to show the involvement of the intellect in those particular propositions in humans. His saying: 'it does not yield except a particular judgment' for it has no way except to particular perception, like this fire at a particular time. So all sense-based judgments are particular judgments obtained by observing the relation of the predicate to the subject, as in the commentary on Ḥikmat al-Ishrāq. His saying: 'so it is derived etc.' i.e. the intellect's derivation when it experiences sensation of the predicate's subsistence for many particulars of the subject, as in al-Muḥākamāt. So it is a primary judgment dependent on repetition of sensation along with grasping the cause, and by this it is distinguished from experimental premises (mujarrabāt) for there is no grasping of the cause in them, although it shares with them. His saying: 'and these are called found etc.' An objection was raised that the found (wijdāniyyāt) are not restricted to rational beings, but occur also in animals, for the perception of hunger, pain, and thirst is indisputably present in them. So there is no meaning to [counting] the found among the observational and then interpreting them as propositions judged by the intellect through sense, external or internal. Unless it is said that what is meant is perception of their occurrence, and this is not present in animals. And it can be said: after conceding the application of 'found' to what occurs in animals, what is counted among the observational is some of the found, so between them is a general-specific relation in some respect. He only said 'and among them is counted what we find in our souls' because sense has no entry into it, except that it is counted among them by way of predominance. His saying: 'As for the judgment that every fire is hot etc.' And it may be said: this universal proposition is from the experimental (mujarrabāt) because its definition applies to it.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the first type of premises: observational premises (al-mushāhadāt). It begins by stating that these are what the intellect judges by mere sense, and divides sense into external and internal. External sense gives judgments like the sun's existence and fire's heat; these are called sensibles. Internal sense gives judgments like having thought or fear; these are called found (wijdāniyyāt). The passage then emphasizes that sense alone yields only particular judgments (e.g., 'this fire is hot'). Universal judgments like 'every fire is hot' come from sensation of many particulars combined with intellectual reasoning. The commentary clarifies that the internal sense may be the estimative faculty (wahm), and that found and observational judgments are related as general and specific: sensibles are observational but not found; inner feelings are found but not observational; and they overlap when known by internal sense. The passage also addresses an objection: animals have found perceptions like hunger, so how can found be limited to rational humans? The answer is that animals perceive occurrence but not the judgment of occurrence, so the found in the technical sense requires intellectual judgment. Finally, it notes that the universal judgment about fire could alternatively be classified as experimental (mujarrabāt) because it involves repetition.

حس

وجدانيات

Misconception: Observational premises are only about external sense objects.
Correction: The author explicitly includes internal sense judgments like thoughts and emotions as observational premises.

A) That we have a thought
B) That fire is hot
C) That we are afraid
D) That we are aware of ourselves

Key Takeaway
Observational premises are immediate sense-based judgments, divided into external sensibles and internal found judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel anger or fear, do you accept those feelings as immediate, undeniable facts? How does acknowledging that these internal states are observational premises shape your understanding of your own soul and its accountability before Allah? Will you begin treating your inner states as direct evidence of your spiritual condition?
?
You rely on external senses daily—seeing the sun, feeling heat—but how often do you rely on your internal sense of self-awareness as a foundational premise for knowing your Creator? If self-awareness is an observational premise, could it be the starting point for a proof of God's existence in your own heart? Are you willing to sit in stillness and let that awareness lead you to Him?

Sense Provides Only Particular Judgments
Explains that sense yields only particular judgments and universal judgments are derived from multiple sensations with reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sense perception alone produces particular judgments and how universal judgments require intellectual processing from repeated sensations.

Core Idea
Sense gives 'this fire is hot', not 'every fire is hot'.

A key principle in kalam methodology: sense experience is always particular. While we perceive individual instances, the universal judgment that 'every fire is hot' involves the intellect deriving a general rule from many particular sensations. This lesson clarifies that distinction and the role of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement that sense yields only particular judgments
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Note the example 'this fire is hot' as a particular judgment.
2
Understand how universal judgments are derived
The author explains that 'every fire is hot' comes from sensing many particular fires along with intellectual reasoning, not from mere sense.
This derivation distinguishes observational premises from experimental premises.
3
Note the exclusion of repetition, intuition, and testimony
The phrase 'by mere sense' excludes judgments that involve repeated observations, intuitive leaps, or reports from others.
This preserves the immediacy of sense.

Source Text

وما نجده بنفوسنا وجدانيات، وليست بمشاهدات ويجتمعان فيما تعلمه بالحس الباطن . قوله : ( واعلم إلخ) المقصود تحقيق أن الحسيات هي القضايا الجزئية دون القضايا الكلية المترتبة عليها، وبيان مدخلية العقل في تلك القضايا الجزئية في الإنسان . قوله : (لا يفيد إلا حكماً جزئياً ) إذ لا سبيل له إلا إلى الإدراك الجزئي كهذه النار في وقت جزئي، فالحسيات كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة بمشاهدة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع، كذا في شرح حكمة الإشراق . قوله: (فمستفاد إلخ) أي استفادة العقل إذا وقع له الإحساس بثبوت المحمول لجزئيات كثيرة من الموضوع كذا في المحاكمات فهو حكم أولي موقوف على تكرر الإحساس مع الوقوف على العلة وبهذا يمتاز عن المجربات فإنه لا وقوف فيها على العلة، وإن كان يشاركها :قوله وتسمى هذه وجدانية إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأن الوجدانيات لا تختص بالعقلاء، بل لعد توجد في البهائم أيضا إذ إدراك الجوع والألم والعطش، مما لا نزاع في حصوله لها، فلا معنى الوجدانيات من المشاهدات ثم تفسيرها بأنها قضايا يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحس، الظاهر أو الباطن اللهم، إلا أن يقال المراد إدراك ،حصولها ، وهذا غير حاصل للبهائم، ويمكن أن يقال : بعد تسليم إطلاق الوجدانيات على الحاصل للبهائم المعدود من المشاهدات بعض الوجدانيات فبينهما عموم من وجه وإنما قال : ويعد منها ما تجده بنفوسنا إذ لا دخل للحس فيه إلا أنه عد منها تغليباً. قوله : ( وأما الحكم بأن كل نار حارة إلخ) وقد يقال : هذه القضية الكلية من المجربات لصدق تعريفها عليها .
The sixth observation – the sixth objective: on premises. ... (third: observational premises) which are (what the intellect judges by mere sense) – the external, like our judgment that the sun exists and that it is luminous, and that fire is hot; these are called sensibles. Or the internal sense, like the judgment that we have thought and that we have fear and anger; these are called found (wijdāniyya) and credal propositions. And among them is counted what we find in our souls not by their instruments, like our awareness of ourselves and of the acts of our selves. Know that sense does not yield except a particular judgment, as in your saying 'this fire is hot'. As for the judgment that every fire is hot, it is derived from sensation of many particulars along with [reason]. His saying: 'by mere sense' i.e. without repetition, intuition, or reports of a group. His saying: 'or the internal sense' – there is disagreement about what this faculty is: is it one of the well-known perceiving faculties or not? The Imam said both views are possible. Then if it is one of them, it appears that it is the estimation (wahm). So the particular corporeal meanings whose perception is by their very occurrence are called found (wijdāniyyāt), and those whose perception is by their likeness are estimative (wahmiyyāt) – thus verified by some researchers in the glosses of the commentary on Mukhtaṣar al-Uṣūl al-‘Aḍudī. His saying: 'and among them is counted etc.' means that between the found (wijdāniyyāt) and the observational there is a general and specific relation in some respect: for the sensibles are observational but not found, and what we find in our souls are found but not observational, and they intersect in what is known by internal sense. His saying: 'Know etc.' The purpose is to verify that sense-based propositions are particular propositions, not the universal propositions that follow from them, and to show the involvement of the intellect in those particular propositions in humans. His saying: 'it does not yield except a particular judgment' for it has no way except to particular perception, like this fire at a particular time. So all sense-based judgments are particular judgments obtained by observing the relation of the predicate to the subject, as in the commentary on Ḥikmat al-Ishrāq. His saying: 'so it is derived etc.' i.e. the intellect's derivation when it experiences sensation of the predicate's subsistence for many particulars of the subject, as in al-Muḥākamāt. So it is a primary judgment dependent on repetition of sensation along with grasping the cause, and by this it is distinguished from experimental premises (mujarrabāt) for there is no grasping of the cause in them, although it shares with them. His saying: 'and these are called found etc.' An objection was raised that the found (wijdāniyyāt) are not restricted to rational beings, but occur also in animals, for the perception of hunger, pain, and thirst is indisputably present in them. So there is no meaning to [counting] the found among the observational and then interpreting them as propositions judged by the intellect through sense, external or internal. Unless it is said that what is meant is perception of their occurrence, and this is not present in animals. And it can be said: after conceding the application of 'found' to what occurs in animals, what is counted among the observational is some of the found, so between them is a general-specific relation in some respect. He only said 'and among them is counted what we find in our souls' because sense has no entry into it, except that it is counted among them by way of predominance. His saying: 'As for the judgment that every fire is hot etc.' And it may be said: this universal proposition is from the experimental (mujarrabāt) because its definition applies to it.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the first type of premises: observational premises (al-mushāhadāt). It begins by stating that these are what the intellect judges by mere sense, and divides sense into external and internal. External sense gives judgments like the sun's existence and fire's heat; these are called sensibles. Internal sense gives judgments like having thought or fear; these are called found (wijdāniyyāt). The passage then emphasizes that sense alone yields only particular judgments (e.g., 'this fire is hot'). Universal judgments like 'every fire is hot' come from sensation of many particulars combined with intellectual reasoning. The commentary clarifies that the internal sense may be the estimative faculty (wahm), and that found and observational judgments are related as general and specific: sensibles are observational but not found; inner feelings are found but not observational; and they overlap when known by internal sense. The passage also addresses an objection: animals have found perceptions like hunger, so how can found be limited to rational humans? The answer is that animals perceive occurrence but not the judgment of occurrence, so the found in the technical sense requires intellectual judgment. Finally, it notes that the universal judgment about fire could alternatively be classified as experimental (mujarrabāt) because it involves repetition.

حكم كلي
تكرار
حدس
مجربات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universal judgments like 'every fire is hot' are directly observed by the senses.
Correction: Sense only gives particular instances; universal judgments require intellectual derivation from repeated sensations.

Checkpoint
What does the author say sense (al-ḥiss) does NOT provide?
A) Particular judgments
B) Universal judgments
C) Both particular and universal
D) No judgments

Key Takeaway
Sense perception is limited to particular judgments; universal judgments require intellectual reasoning from multiple sensations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that 'God is just' or 'charity is good', are you relying on direct sensory experience or on repeated observations and reasoning? How certain are you of that distinction, and what does it say about the basis of your faith? Commit today to examining one such belief and tracing its origin.
?
Think of a universal statement you hold about people—like 'everyone is selfish' or 'all humans are fallible'. Did you personally observe all cases, or did you form this by reasoning from a few experiences? How might this lesson warn you against mistaking a particular judgment for a universal truth?
?
The passage teaches that mere sense gives only particulars, not universals. In your daily life, when do you treat a single experience as if it were a universal rule? What would change if you paused to ask: 'Have I observed enough, or am I leaping to a general conclusion?'

The Internal Sense Faculty and Wijdāniyyāt
Discusses the internal sense faculty, the relation between wijdāniyyāt and mushāhadāt, and responds to an objection about animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the internal sense (al-ḥiss al-bāṭin) and how wijdāniyyāt differ from mushāhadāt, including the objection about animals.

Core Idea
Internal sense yields found judgments (wijdāniyyāt), which overlap but differ from observational premises.

This lesson delves deeper into the internal sense that was introduced earlier. The author discusses what this faculty is—likely the estimation (wahm)—and explains the relationship between the found (wijdāniyyāt) and the observational (mushāhadāt). An important objection about animals having wijdāniyyāt is addressed. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion on the internal sense faculty
Pay attention to the identification of internal sense with wahm and the distinction between wijdāniyyāt and wahmiyyāt.
2
Understand the relationship between wijdāniyyāt and mushāhadāt
The passage explains a general-specific relation: sensibles are observational but not found; inner feelings are found but not observational; they intersect in internal sense judgments.
3
Consider the objection about animals and the response
Animals have feelings like hunger, so how can wijdāniyyāt be restricted to humans? The response distinguishes between mere perception and intellectual judgment.
This clarifies the technical meaning of wijdāniyyāt.

المرصد السادس - المقصد السادس : في المقدمات بمتساويين، وكل منقسم بمتساويين زوج ( الثالثة المشاهدات ) وهي ( ما يحكم به العقل بمجرد الحس) الظاهر مثل حكمنا بوجود الشمس وكونها مضيئة، وكون النار حارة وتسمى Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wijdāniyyāt are the same as mushāhadāt because both involve sense.
Correction: They overlap but are distinct: sensibles are observational but not found, inner feelings are found but not observational, and only some judgments (from internal sense) belong to both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson distinguishes between animal perception and human judgment. In what areas of your daily life do you react like an animal—only sensing—rather than engaging your intellect to judge and derive meaning? What would change if you deliberately brought intellectual awareness to every feeling that arises today?
?

When will you commit to distinguishing genuine wijdāniyyāt from baseless wahmiyyāt in your thoughts?

Experimental Premises and the Hidden Syllogism
Defines experimental premises and explains the hidden syllogism behind them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of experimental premises (mujarrabat) and the hidden syllogism that grounds their certainty.

Core Idea
Experimental premises uncover causes through repeated observation, guided by a hidden rational syllogism.

This passage delves into the fourth type of rational premise: what we learn through repeated sense experience. But as Sanusi explains, mere repetition is not enough—there is a hidden syllogism at work that transforms observed patterns into certain causal judgments. Let's examine how this process works and how it differs from simple induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic definition and its translation. Notice that the intellect judges 'by means of sense with repetition'—both sense and repetition are necessary.
The definition is compact; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the hidden syllogism
The passage states that a hidden syllogism is necessary. Look at how it reasons: repeated uniform occurrence implies a cause, even if the cause's nature is unknown. This is the core of experimental reasoning.
The syllogism is 'hidden' because the person making the judgment may not be consciously aware of it.
3
Understand the causal inference
Once the cause is known to exist, the effect is judged with certainty. The examples of hitting wood (pain) and drinking scammony (purgation) illustrate this cause-effect link.
4
Distinguish from other premises
The commentary later distinguishes experimental premises from induction (no syllogism), intuitive premises (syllogism not from repetition), and propositions with inherent syllogisms (syllogism attached to both terms).
This clarifies the unique nature of mujarrabat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوقف على العلة فلعل الإحساسات الجزئية تعد النفس لقبول العقد الكلي من المبدأ الفياض، ولا شك تلك الإحساسات إنما تؤدي إلى اليقين إذا كانت صائبة فلولا أن العقل يميز بين الحق والباطل من الإحساسات، لم يتميز الصواب عن ( الرابعة المجربات ) وهي ( ما يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحس مع التكرار ) ولا بد مع ذلك من قياس خفي هو أن الوقوع المتكرر على نهج واحد دائماً أو أكثرياً، لم يكن اتفاقياً بل لا بد أن يكون هناك سبب، وإن لم يعرف ماهية ذلك السبب، وإذا علم حصول ذلك السبب حكم بوجود المسبب قطعاً، وذلك مثل حكمنا بأن الضرب بالخشب مؤلم وبأن شرب السقمونيا مسهل (الخامسة الحدسيات وهي قضايا مبدأ الحكم بها حدس قوله: (فلولا أن العقل إلخ) فلاجل هذا التمييز كان للعقل مدخل في الحسيات، ولعدم هذا التمييز في الحيوانات العجم، كانت الأحكام الحسية منها بمجرد الحس، ولا يترتب عليها الأحكام الكلية، فإن قيل : إذا لم تكن الأحكام الكلية حاصلة للحيوانات، كيف نهرب عن كل نار بعد إحساسها لنار مخصوصة؟ قلت : ذلك لعدم التمييز بين الأمثال لا للحكم الكلي . قوله : ( من قياس خفي أي قياس مترتب لا يشعر به صاحب الحكم مع حصوله، وذلك القياس حاصل من تكرار المشاهدة، وبهذا يمتاز عن الأحكام الاستقرائية، إذ لا قياس فيها وعن الحدسيات لأن القياس المترتب فيها غير حاصل من تكرر المشاهدة وعن قضايا قياساتها معها لأن القياس فيها لازم للطرفين. قوله: (لم يكن (اتفاقياً) أي حاصلاً بمجرد توافقه بمجرد توافقه مع ذلك الشيء في الوجود، بسببه من أن يكون ذلك الشيء بنفسه أو بجزئه أو بلازمه سبباً له . قوله : ( وذلك مثل حكمنا إلخ أورد مثالين من قبيل الفعل إشارة إلى أن المجربات لا تكون إلا من قبيل التأثير والتأثر فلا يقال : جربنا أن السواد هيئة قارة .
The stop on the cause: perhaps the particular sensations prepare the soul to accept the universal knot from the overflowing principle. And there is no doubt that those sensations lead to certainty only if they are correct. For if the intellect did not distinguish between truth and falsehood among sensations, the correct would not be distinguished from the erroneous. (The fourth: experimental premises) which are (what the intellect judges by means of sense with repetition). And with that there must be a hidden syllogism: that repeated occurrence in one way always or mostly is not accidental, but there must be a cause, even if the nature of that cause is not known. And when the occurrence of that cause is known, one judges absolutely the existence of the effect. That is like our judgment that hitting with wood is painful and that drinking scammony is purgative. His saying: 'If not for the intellect etc.' So because of this distinction, the intellect has an entry into sense-based judgments, and due to the lack of this distinction in mute animals, their sense-based judgments are by mere sense, and universal judgments do not follow from them. If it is said: if animals do not have universal judgments, how do they flee from every fire after sensing a particular fire? I say: that is due to lack of distinction between similarities, not due to a universal judgment. His saying: 'from a hidden syllogism' i.e. a syllogism composed without the person being aware of it while it occurs. And that syllogism arises from repetition of observation, and by this it is distinguished from inductive judgments, for there is no syllogism in them, and from intuitive premises (ḥadsiyyāt), because the composed syllogism in them does not arise from repetition of observation, and from propositions whose syllogisms are with them, because the syllogism in them is necessary for both sides. His saying: 'it was not accidental' i.e. occurring merely by its coinciding with that thing in existence, but because that thing itself, or its part, or its concomitant is a cause for it. His saying: 'and that is like our judgment etc.' He gave two examples from the category of action, indicating that experimental premises only occur in the category of influence and being influenced, so it is not said: we have experienced that blackness is a stable quality.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines and explains experimental premises (al-mujarrabāt). It begins by continuing the previous discussion on the role of intellect in distinguishing correct sensations. Then it formally defines the fourth type of premise: what the intellect judges by sense with repetition. The key element is a hidden syllogism: repeated occurrence in a uniform manner (always or mostly) indicates that it is not accidental but has a cause, even if the cause's nature is unknown. Once the cause is known to exist, the effect is judged with certainty. Examples: hitting wood causes pain, and drinking scammony causes purgation. The commentary then clarifies that the intellect's entry into sense-based judgments is what distinguishes humans from animals; animals flee fire due to lack of distinction between similar instances, not due to a universal judgment. The hidden syllogism is distinguished from induction (no syllogism), intuitive premises (syllogism not from repetition), and propositions with inherent syllogisms (syllogism attached to both terms). Finally, it notes that experimental premises are limited to cases of cause and effect (influence and being influenced), not static qualities like blackness.

Correction: The passage emphasizes that experimental premises require a hidden syllogism that infers a cause from uniform recurrence, which induction lacks.

Definition and Explanation of Intuitive Premises (al-Hadsiyyat)
Defines intuitive premises and addresses an objection about causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what intuitive premises (ḥadsiyyāt) are, how they differ from experimental premises, and why the objection about accidents does not undermine the role of causality.

Core Idea
Intuitive premises arise from a single insight, not repeated observation.

This lesson introduces the fifth category of certain premises: intuitive premises (al-ḥadsiyyāt). The author defines them concisely and notes their similarity to experimental premises. The commentary then clarifies a potential misunderstanding about accidental occurrences, reinforcing that regular correlations point to real causes even if their nature is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definition and the objection-and-answer structure.
Notice that the definition is very brief; the commentary expands it.
2
Understand the definition of intuitive premises
Intuitive premises are propositions whose judgment originates from intuition (ḥads) without needing repeated observation. Contrast this with experimental premises that rely on repetition.
Al-Ṭūsī compares them to experimental premises in their effect.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection suggests that calling something 'not accidental' implies accidents have no cause. The answer clarifies that accidents do have causes, but they are unknown; the phrase means the effect is not a coincidence but stems from a cause in the substance.
This discussion actually pertains to experimental premises but clarifies the concept of causality.
4
Reflect on the importance of causality
Consider how this reasoning guards against skepticism: regular correlations (always or mostly) indicate a real cause, even if its specific nature is unknown.

(الخامسة الحدسيات وهي قضايا مبدأ الحكم بها حدس في الاحتياج إلى تكرار المشاهدة ولذا قال المحقق الطوسي في شرح الإشارات: إنها تجري مجرى المجربات . قوله: (الحدسيات إلخ ) لم يعرفها لظهور تعريفها من نفس اللفظ أعني المنسوبة إلى الحدس بمعنى السرعة في السير، ولذا عرفه البعض تسامحاً بسرعة الانتقال من المبادي إلى المطالب. قوله: (لم يكن اتفاقيا بل لا بد إلخ) فإن قلت : هذا يشعر بأن الاتفاقيات لا سبب لها مع أن المصرح به خلافه فإن لها أسباباً قطعاً لكنها غير معلومة، قلت: ليس المعنى ما فهمت، بل المراد أنه إذا ترتب على شرب السقمونيا الإسهال ترتباً دائمياً، أو أكثرياً يحكم العقل بأن في السقمونيا سبباً للإسهال، وإن لم يعلم أنه حرارته أو برودته أو نحو ذلك، وأنه لم يتحقق الإسهال معه بطريق الاتفاق أي بأن اتفق مقارنته لشربه من غير إن نشأ من السقمونيا نفسه، بل من شيء آخر اتفق تحققه مع الشرب.
(The fifth: intuitive premises) which are propositions whose judgment’s starting point is intuition (ḥads) without needing repetition of observation. Hence the investigator al-Ṭūsī said in his commentary on al-Ishārāt: they run in the course of experimental premises. His saying: 'The intuitive etc.' He did not define them because their definition is clear from the word itself, i.e., attributed to ḥads meaning speed in travel. Hence some defined it tolerantly as quick transition from principles to desired conclusions. His saying: 'it was not accidental but rather necessary etc.' For if you say: this suggests that accidents have no cause, whereas the explicit statement is contrary: they certainly have causes but they are unknown. I say: the meaning is not what you understood. Rather, the intended meaning is that if purgation follows the drinking of scammony always or mostly, the intellect judges that there is a cause in scammony for purgation, even if it does not know whether it is its heat, coldness, or the like, and that the purgation did not occur with it by way of coincidence – i.e., that it happened to accompany its drinking without arising from the scammony itself, but from something else that happened to occur with the drinking.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines intuitive premises (al-ḥadsiyyāt). The definition is given concisely: propositions whose judgment originates from intuition (ḥads) without needing repeated observation. The commentator notes that al-Ṭūsī likened them to experimental premises in their effect. Then the commentator explains that the term 'intuitive' is clear from the word itself: ḥads means speed, so it refers to quick inference from principles to conclusions. The passage then addresses an objection regarding the statement 'it was not accidental': someone might think that accidents have no cause, but the commentator clarifies that the intention is not that accidents lack causes, but rather that when a regular correlation (like scammony causing purgation) occurs always or mostly, the intellect judges a causal connection, and that the effect is not a mere coincidence. The cause may be unknown in its specific nature, but its existence is inferred. This discussion actually pertains to the previous category (experimental), but it is included here as part of the commentary on the overall topic.

Al-Mujarrabat: The Hidden Syllogism in Empirical Premises
Defines mujarrabat, explains the hidden syllogism, and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical premises (mujarrabat) involve a hidden syllogism that infers causality from repeated observation, and how this distinguishes them from purely sensory or inductive judgments.

Core Idea
Empirical knowledge relies on a hidden syllogism that assumes repeated patterns have a cause.

This lesson examines the fourth category of rational judgments: empirical premises (al-mujarrabat). The author explains that while these judgments are based on repeated sensory experience, they are not purely empirical—they involve an implicit rational inference. You will see how the intellect, through a hidden syllogism, posits a cause behind regular occurrences, and how this distinguishes empirical knowledge from animal sensation and induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before interpretation.
Notice that the passage includes both the original text and the commentator's elaboration.
2
Grasp the definition of mujarrabat
Identify the definition: judgments made by the intellect via the senses with repetition. Note the mention of a hidden syllogism.
The hidden syllogism is central to the concept.
3
Explore the hidden syllogism and its implications
Understand the logic: repeated occurrence always or mostly indicates a cause, leading to a definite judgment. The cause may be unknown in essence but its effect is known.
Reflect on examples: striking wood causes pain, scammony causes purging.
4
Consider the objections and distinctions
Pay attention to the distinction between human and animal judgments, and how empirical premises differ from induction and intuition. Also note the clarification about coincidental events.
The passage addresses potential misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابعة المجربات ) وهي ( ما يحكم بها العقل بواسطة الحس مع التكرار ) ولا بد مع ذلك من قياس خفي هو أن الوقوع المتكرر على نهج واحد دائماً أو أكثرياً، لم يكن اتفاقياً بل لا بد أن يكون هناك سبب، وإن لم يعرف ماهية ذلك السبب، وإذا علم حصول ذلك السبب حكم بوجود المسبب قطعاً، وذلك مثل حكمنا بأن الضرب بالخشب مؤلم وبأن شرب السقمونيا مسهل قوله: (فلولا أن العقل إلخ) فلاجل هذا التمييز كان للعقل مدخل في الحسيات، ولعدم هذا التمييز في الحيوانات العجم، كانت الأحكام الحسية منها بمجرد الحس، ولا يترتب عليها الأحكام الكلية، فإن قيل : إذا لم تكن الأحكام الكلية حاصلة للحيوانات، كيف نهرب عن كل نار بعد إحساسها لنار مخصوصة؟ قلت : ذلك لعدم التمييز بين الأمثال لا للحكم الكلي . قوله : ( من قياس خفي أي قياس مترتب لا يشعر به صاحب الحكم مع حصوله، وذلك القياس حاصل من تكرار المشاهدة، وبهذا يمتاز عن الأحكام الاستقرائية، إذ لا قياس فيها وعن الحدسيات لأن القياس المترتب فيها غير حاصل من تكرر المشاهدة وعن قضايا قياساتها معها لأن القياس فيها لازم للطرفين. قوله: (لم يكن (اتفاقياً) أي حاصلاً بمجرد توافقه بمجرد توافقه مع ذلك الشيء في الوجود، بسببه من أن يكون ذلك الشيء بنفسه أو بجزئه أو بلازمه سبباً له . قوله : ( وذلك مثل حكمنا إلخ أورد مثالين من قبيل الفعل إشارة إلى أن المجربات لا تكون إلا من قبيل التأثير والتأثر فلا يقال : جربنا أن السواد هيئة قارة . قوله: (لم يكن اتفاقيا بل لا بد إلخ) فإن قلت : هذا يشعر بأن الاتفاقيات لا سبب لها مع أن المصرح به خلافه فإن لها أسباباً قطعاً لكنها غير معلومة، قلت: ليس المعنى ما فهمت، بل المراد أنه إذا ترتب على شرب السقمونيا الإسهال ترتباً دائمياً، أو أكثرياً يحكم العقل بأن في السقمونيا سبباً للإسهال، وإن لم يعلم أنه حرارته أو برودته أو نحو ذلك، وأنه لم يتحقق الإسهال معه بطريق الاتفاق أي بأن اتفق مقارنته لشربه من غير إن نشأ من السقمونيا نفسه، بل من شيء آخر اتفق تحققه مع الشرب.
(The fourth: empirical premises) They are (what the intellect judges by means of sense with repetition). And along with that there must be a hidden syllogism, namely that repeated occurrence in one manner, always or mostly, is not coincidental, but rather there must be a cause, even if the essence of that cause is not known. And when the occurrence of that cause is known, one judges the existence of the effect definitively. Such as our judgment that striking wood is painful and that drinking scammony is a laxative. His saying: ('Were it not for the intellect etc.') Because of this distinction, the intellect has an entry in sense perceptions, and due to the absence of this distinction in dumb animals, their sensory judgments are by mere sense, and universal judgments are not derived from them. If it is said: If universal judgments are not obtained for animals, how do they flee from every fire after sensing a particular fire? I say: That is due to the lack of distinction between similars, not due to a universal judgment. His saying: ('From a hidden syllogism') i.e., a sequential syllogism of which the possessor of the judgment is unaware at its occurrence. That syllogism is obtained from repeated observation. And by this it is distinguished from inductive judgments, for there is no syllogism in them, and from intuitive judgments because the sequential syllogism in them is not obtained from repeated observation, and from propositions whose syllogisms are with them because the syllogism in them is necessary for both sides. His saying: (was not coincidental) i.e., occurring merely by its coinciding with that thing in existence, such that that thing itself or its part or its concomitant is a cause for it. His saying: ('And that is like our judgment etc.') He gave two examples from the category of action to indicate that empirical premises are only from the category of influence and being influenced, so it is not said: we have experienced that blackness is a fixed state. His saying: ('was not coincidental but rather there must etc.') If you say: This suggests that coincidences have no cause, while the declared view is the opposite, for they certainly have causes but they are unknown, I say: The meaning is not what you understood, but rather the intended meaning is that if laxative follows drinking scammony persistently or mostly, the intellect judges that in scammony there is a cause for laxation, even if it does not know whether it is its heat or cold or something else, and that the laxation did not occur with it by way of coincidence, i.e., that it coincidentally accompanied its drinking without arising from scammony itself, but from something else that coincidentally occurred with the drinking.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines empirical premises (al-mujarrabat) and explains the hidden syllogism that underlies them. It begins with the definition: judgments made by the intellect through the senses with repetition. Then it states the necessity of a hidden syllogism: repeated occurrence always or mostly indicates a cause, even if the cause's nature is unknown. Examples are given. The commentary then addresses several points: first, the role of reason in distinguishing true from false sensations, and why animals do not derive universal judgments. Second, it explains the hidden syllogism as a sequential reasoning of which the judge is unaware, distinguishing it from induction (no syllogism) and intuition (syllogism not from repeated observation). Third, it clarifies 'coincidental' as mere concurrence without causation. Fourth, it addresses an objection: if coincidences have unknown causes, does the argument imply they have no cause? The answer is no; the point is that regular occurrence forces the intellect to posit a cause, not that coincidences are uncaused.

القياس الخفي
الاتفاقيات
السببية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical premises are purely sensory judgments without any rational inference.
Correction: They actually involve a hidden syllogism where the intellect infers a cause from repeated observation.

Checkpoint

B) Inductive judgments require repeated observation, while empirical premises do not.
C) Empirical premises are based on single observations, while inductive judgments require many.
D) There is no difference; they are the same.

Key Takeaway
Empirical premises are not mere sensory reports; they depend on a hidden syllogism that assumes repeated regularities have a cause, thus involving rational inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says animals lack universal judgments—they only recognize similarity. Is your hidden syllogism sound, or could you be mistaking coincidence for causality? How will you test it today?

Al-Hadsiyyat: Intuitive Premises and Their Distinction from Empirical
Defines hadsiyyat with examples and distinguishes from mujarrabat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what intuitive premises (hadsiyyat) are, how they differ from empirical premises (mujarrabat), and why they require a strong intuition and repeated observation.

Core Idea
Intuitive premises reveal causes through strong insight, not mere experience.

In this passage, the author defines intuitive premises—propositions whose certainty stems from a strong intuitive leap. Through examples like inferring the Maker's knowledge from His perfect acts, he distinguishes them from empirical premises, where the cause's essence remains unknown. This lesson unpacks the definition, the role of repeated observation, and the key difference in causal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the definition and examples.
2
Identify the definition and examples
Locate the definition of hadsiyyat and the two examples given. Notice how each example involves a strong intuition.
The first example is about the Maker; the second about the moon's light.
3
Understand the requirement of repeated observation
Note that intuitive premises require repeated observation and a hidden syllogism, similar to empirical premises.
The text says 'لا بد في الحدسيات من تكرار المشاهدة ومقارنة القياس الخفي كما في المجربات'.
4
Grasp the distinction between hadsiyyat and mujarrabat
The key difference: in empirical premises, the cause's causality is known but its essence is unknown; in intuitive premises, both causality and essence are known.
This results in different numbers of accompanying syllogisms.
5
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary clarifies that strong intuition is necessary for certainty, and that some intuitions are conjectural. Consider the debate on whether repeated observation is always needed.
Qutb al-Din al-Razi is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الخامسة الحدسيات وهي قضايا مبدأ الحكم بها حدس قوي يزول معه الشك ) كعلم الصانع بإتقان فعله فإنا لما شاهدنا أن أفعاله تعالى محكمة متقنة حكمنا بأنه عالم حكماً حدسياً وكذا لما شاهدنا اختلاف حال القمر في تشكلاته النورية بحسب اختلاف أوضاعه من الشمس حدسنا منه أن نوره من نورها، ولا بد في الحدسيات من تكرار المشاهدة ومقارنة القياس الخفي كما في المجربات والفرق بينهما أن السبب في المجربات معلوم السببية مجهول الماهية ، فلذلك كان القياس المقارن لها قياساً واحداً، وهو أنه لو لم يكن لعلة لم يكن دائماً ولا أكثرياً، وأن السبب في الحدسيات معلوم السببية، والماهية معاً فلذلك كان المقارن لها أقيسة مختلفة بحسب اختلاف العلل في ماهياتها قوله : ( حدس قوي إلخ ) فلو لم يكن الحدس بهذه المرتبة لا يكون من القطعيات، ولذا عدها البعض من الظنيات. قوله : ( ولا بد في الحدسيات أي التي يحكم فيها العقل بمعونة الحس كما في المثالين المذكورين، وأما الحدسيات العقلية فلا مشاهدة فيها فضلاً عن تكرارها، ولذا قال في شرح التجريد : الجديد أن الحدس قد يحصل بتكرر المشاهدة، والمقصود من هذا الكلام إبداء الفارق بين المجربات والحدسيات التي يحكم فيها بمعونة الحس. قوله : ( الخامس الحدسيات إلخ) وقد تكون الحدسيات الظنيات لا من الضروريات القطعية، وإلا لما جوز العقل نقيضها، والعقل يجوز في المثال المشهور أن يكون نور القمر من أمر يدور اختلافه مع اختلاف القرب والبعد . قوله : ( ولا بد في الحدسيات من تكرار المشاهدة قد يمنع توقف كل حدس على تكرار المشاهدة كما في مشاهدة الصفة المتقنة، ويؤيده ما ذكره قطب الدين الرازي في شرح من أنه إما أن يحتاج العقل في الجزم إلى تكرار المشاهدة مرة بعد أخرى، أو لا يحتاج فإن احتاج فهي المجربات، وإن لم يحتج فهي الحدسيات، وقد يجاب بأن وقوع المتقن من غير العالم نادرا اتفاقياً مما لا شبهة في جوازه، وهذا على تقدير تسليمه يدفع المثال المخصوص، ولا يدفع
(The fifth: intuitive premises) They are propositions whose judgment begins with a strong intuition that removes doubt, like knowledge of the Maker from the perfection of His act. For when we see that His acts, exalted, are precise and perfect, we judge that He is knowing by an intuitive judgment. Similarly, when we see the varying state of the moon in its luminous formations according to its different positions relative to the sun, we intuit that its light is from the sun's light. And in intuitive premises there must be repeated observation and comparison with the hidden syllogism, as in empirical premises. And the difference between them is that the cause in empirical premises is known in terms of causality but unknown in essence. Therefore, the syllogism accompanying them is one syllogism, namely: if it were not for a cause, it would not be always or mostly. And the cause in intuitive premises is known in terms of causality and essence together. Therefore, the accompanying syllogisms are multiple, differing according to the differences in the essences of the causes. His saying: ('A strong intuition etc.') If the intuition were not of this rank, it would not be among certainties, so some consider them among conjectural matters. His saying: ('And there must be in intuitive premises') i.e., those in which the intellect judges with the aid of sense, as in the two mentioned examples. As for purely intellectual intuitions, there is no observation in them, let alone its repetition. Hence it was said in Sharh al-Tajrid: The new view is that intuition may occur through repeated observation. The purpose of this speech is to show the difference between empirical and intuitive premises in which judgment is made with the aid of sense. His saying: ('The fifth: intuitive premises etc.') And intuitive premises may be conjectural, not among necessary certainties, otherwise the intellect would not allow their opposite. The intellect does allow in the famous example that the moon's light could be from something whose variation depends on proximity and distance. His saying: ('And there must be in intuitive premises from repeated observation') It may be denied that every intuition depends on repeated observation, as in observing a perfect quality. This is supported by what Qutb al-Din al-Razi mentioned in his commentary: either the intellect in its certainty needs repeated observation time after time, or it does not. If it needs, then it is empirical; if it does not need, then it is intuitive. It may be answered that the occurrence of something perfect from someone who is not knowledgeable is rarely coincidental, with no doubt about its permissibility. This, if granted, refutes the particular example but does not refute.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines intuitive premises (al-hadsiyyat) and distinguishes them from empirical premises. It begins with the definition: propositions whose judgment starts with a strong intuition removing doubt. Two examples are given: knowing the Maker from the perfection of His acts, and inferring the moon's light from the sun. The passage then states that intuition requires repeated observation and a hidden syllogism, similar to empirical premises. The key difference is that in empirical premises, the cause's causality is known but its essence unknown, leading to a single syllogism: if no cause, then not always/mostly. In intuitive premises, both causality and essence are known, leading to multiple syllogisms based on the essences of the causes. The commentary elaborates: the intuition must be strong to yield certainty; otherwise it is merely conjectural. It distinguishes sense-aided intuition (which requires observation) from purely intellectual intuition (no observation). It cites a view that intuition can arise from repeated observation. The commentary also notes that intuitive premises can be conjectural, as the intellect can entertain the opposite. Finally, it debates whether all intuition needs repeated observation, presenting Qutb al-Din al-Razi's criterion and a possible counterexample.

Correction: The passage states that sense-aided intuitive premises require repeated observation, though purely intellectual intuitions do not.

Checkpoint

B) In empirical premises the cause's essence is unknown, but in intuitive premises it is known.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive premises are certain judgments reached through a strong intuition that makes the cause's both causality and essence known, unlike empirical premises where the cause's essence remains unknown.

Intuitive vs. Experiential Judgments
Defines intuitive judgments and distinguishes them from experiential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of intuitive judgments and how they differ from experiential judgments in terms of causal knowledge.

Core Idea
Intuitive judgments reveal the cause's essence; experiential judgments only confirm its existence.

After establishing the categories of rational judgments, al-Sanusi turns to a specific type: intuitive judgments (hadsiyyat). These are judgments reached through a hidden syllogism based on repeated observation. This passage defines them and distinguishes them from experiential judgments (mujarrabat) by exploring the nature of the cause in each case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two examples and the logical structure of the distinction.
The passage begins mid-sentence; the opening word 'قوي' refers to a strong judgment.
2
Identify the definition of intuitive judgments
Find the core definition: a strong insight that removes doubt, achieved through a hidden syllogism and repetition of observation.
The word 'حدسيات' itself means intuitive judgments.
3
Examine the two examples
The first example is knowledge of God's wisdom from the perfection of His acts. The second is inferring the moon's light source from its phases. See how each involves a hidden syllogism.
Both examples rely on repeated observation and a causal inference.
4
Understand the requirement of repetition and hidden syllogism
Just like experiential judgments, intuitive judgments need takrar al-mushahada (repetition of observation) and a qiyas khafi (hidden syllogism).
5
Grasp the key distinction
In experiential judgments, the cause is known in its causality but not its quiddity (essence). In intuitive judgments, both the causality and the quiddity are known. This difference determines the type of syllogism used.
This is the main point that separates hadsiyyat from mujarrabat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوي يزول معه الشك كعلم الصانع بإتقان فعله فإنا لما شاهدنا أن أفعاله تعالى محكمة متقنة حكمنا بأنه عالم حكماً حدسياً وكذا لما شاهدنا اختلاف حال القمر في تشكلاته النورية بحسب اختلاف أوضاعه من الشمس حدسنا منه أن نوره من نورها، ولا بد في الحدسيات من تكرار المشاهدة ومقارنة القياس الخفي كما في المجربات والفرق بينهما أن السبب في المجربات معلوم السببية مجهول الماهية، فلذلك كان القياس المقارن لها قياساً واحداً، وهو أنه لو لم يكن لعلة لم يكن دائماً ولا أكثرياً، وأن السبب في الحدسيات معلوم السببية، والماهية معاً فلذلك كان المقارن لها أقيسة مختلفة بحسب اختلاف العلل في ماهياتها
A strong [insight] by which doubt is removed, like knowledge of the Maker through the perfection of His act: for we, when we observe that His acts, exalted is He, are precise and perfect, judge that He is knowing by an intuitive judgment. Likewise, when we observe the variation of the moon's states in its luminous formations according to the variation of its positions relative to the sun, we intuit from it that its light is from its light. And in intuitive judgments, repetition of observation and comparison of a hidden syllogism is necessary, as in experiential judgments. The difference between them is that the cause in experiential judgments is known in its causality but unknown in its quiddity; therefore the syllogism compared to it is a single syllogism, namely that if it were not for a cause, it would not be perpetual nor most often. And the cause in intuitive judgments is known in its causality and quiddity together; therefore the syllogisms compared to it are different according to the difference of causes in their quiddities.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines intuitive judgments (hadsiyyat) and distinguishes them from experiential judgments (mujarrabat). The text begins with the defining characteristic: a strong insight that removes doubt. It provides two examples: knowledge of God's wisdom from the perfection of His acts, and knowledge that the moon's light is from the sun by observing its phases. The passage then states that intuitive judgments require repeated observation and a hidden syllogism, just like experiential judgments. The key distinction is that in experiential judgments, the cause is known in its causality (we know it has a cause) but unknown in its essence (what exactly the cause is), so only one syllogism applies: if there were no cause, the effect would not occur. In contrast, in intuitive judgments, both the causality and the quiddity of the cause are known, so multiple syllogisms are used depending on the nature of the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think intuitive judgments are purely rational without any empirical input.
Correction: The passage states that intuitive judgments require repetition of observation (takrar al-mushahada), hence they rely on empirical data combined with a hidden syllogism.

Checkpoint

B) In intuitive judgments, both the causality and the quiddity of the cause are known; in experiential, only the causality is known.
C) Experiential judgments are stronger than intuitive judgments.
D) Intuitive judgments are always certain, while experiential are probable.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive judgments require repeated observation and a hidden syllogism, and they differ from experiential judgments in that both the causality and the quiddity of the cause are known.

Definition and Conditions of Traditions (Mutawatirat)
Defines traditions and discusses the condition of number and reliance on sense-perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of traditions (mutawatirat) as a source of certainty, why a fixed number of reporters is not required, and the essential conditions that traditions must be based on sense-perception and yield particular knowledge.

Core Idea
Traditions yield certainty through reports that cannot be collusive, not through a fixed number of reporters.

This passage introduces the sixth category of certain knowledge: traditions. It defines them as knowledge obtained from reports of a group that cannot conspire to lie. The author also refutes the idea of a fixed number of reporters, explaining that the criterion is the attainment of certainty. Finally, he outlines the conditions for traditions, including repetition, a hidden syllogism, and reliance on sensory observation, leading to particular knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the definition and the examples provided.
The examples 'Mecca and Galen' are likely famous cases of knowledge from traditions.
2
Identify the key components
Find the definition of traditions, the refutation of a fixed number, and the conditions mentioned. List them in your own words.
The passage moves from definition to refutation to conditions and then to the nature of the resulting knowledge.
3
Reflect on the condition of certainty
Consider why the author insists that the number of reporters is not fixed but depends on when certainty is achieved. How does this differ from common assumptions?
This is a key point against those who require a specific count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( السادسة المتواترات ) وهي ( ما يحكم بها بمجرد خبر جماعة يمتنع تواطؤهم على الكذب ) مكة وجالينوس، ومن اعتبر في التواتر عدداً معيناً، فقد أحال فإن بوجود ذلك مما يختلف بحسب الوقائع والضابط مبلغ ما يقع معه اليقين، فإذا حصل اليقين فقد تم العدد ولا بد في المتواترات من تكرار وقياس خفي وأن تكون مستندة إلى المشاهدة، فيكون الحاصل من التواتر علماً جزئياً من شأنه أن يحصل بالإحساس فلذلك لا يقع في العلوم بالذات كالمحسوسات
(The sixth: traditions) and they are (that which is judged merely by the report of a group whose collusion in lying is impossible) [like] Mecca and Galen. And whoever considers a specific number for tradition has erred, for its existence differs according to circumstances, and the standard is the amount that yields certainty. When certainty is obtained, the number is complete. And in traditions, there must be repetition and a hidden syllogism, and that they be based on observation. Then what results from tradition is particular knowledge whose nature is to be obtained by sensation, therefore it does not occur in the sciences essentially, like sensations.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines traditions (mutawatirat) as the sixth category of certain knowledge. It states that traditions are judgments made solely on the basis of a report from a group whose collusion in lying is impossible. Examples given are knowledge of the existence of Mecca and Galen (likely famous examples). The text then refutes the view that a specific number of reporters is required for tawatur; rather, the number varies depending on the case, and the criterion is the attainment of certainty—once certainty is achieved, the number is sufficient. It also states that traditions require repetition (of reports) and a hidden syllogism, and that they must be based on sense-perception (observation). Consequently, the knowledge gained from tradition is particular knowledge (juz'i) that is obtained through sensation, and therefore it does not occur essentially in the sciences (which deal with universals), similar to sense-perceptions.

Correction: The number is not fixed; it varies until certainty is achieved.

Key Takeaway

Estimative Judgments in Sensible Matters
Defines estimative judgments in sensible matters and notes their truthfulness and agreement with intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that estimative judgments (wahmiyyat) in sensible matters are considered certain because they are affirmed by the intellect and form the basis of sciences like geometry.

Core Idea
Estimative judgments in sensibles are true and intellect confirms them.

This lesson focuses on the seventh type of certain judgments: estimative judgments as they apply to sensible things. The passage explains that these judgments are truthful and provides a concrete example. It also highlights the agreement between the estimative faculty and the intellect, which grounds certain sciences such as geometry.

The Seventh Certain Premise: Imaginal Judgments in Sensory Matters
Defines the seventh type of certain premises: wahmiyyat in sensory matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why imaginal judgments in sensory matters are considered certain, and what limits their absolute truthfulness.

Core Idea
Imaginal judgments are certain when reason confirms them, but not absolutely.

This lesson introduces the seventh category of certain premises: imaginal judgments (al-wahmiyyat) applied to sensory objects. The author explains that these judgments are considered certain because reason affirms them, but he also warns that they are not always absolutely true. We will look at the definition, the example, and the important qualification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: definition, justification, and qualification.
This is a dense passage; take your time.
2
Identify the definition
Find where the author defines imaginal judgments (الوهميات) in sensory matters. What is the key example given?
The example is 'every body is in a direction'.
3
Understand the justification
Why are these judgments considered certain? Notice the role of reason (العقل) in confirming the imagination's judgments.
The passage says reason affirms them and they match sensory experience.
4
Note the qualification
The commentary explains that 'true' means 'in general' (في الجملة). Why is this important? What counterexample is given?
Imagination can be wrong in particular cases, like imagining enmity.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Summarize in your own words: when are imaginal judgments certain, and when are they not?
This connects to the broader framework of certain premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(السابعة الوهميات في المحسوسات) فإن حكم الوهم في الأمور المحسوسة صادق نحو "كل جسم في جهة" فإن العقل يصدقه أحكامه على المحسوسات ولتطابقهما كانت العلوم الجارية مجرى الهندسيات.
قوله: (الوهميات) لم يعرفها لما مر في الحدسيات.
قوله: (فإن حكم الوهم إلخ) تعليل للحكم المقدر أي إنما عد الوهميات في المحسوسات من القطعية، فإن حكم الوهم إلخ سواء كان جزئياً نحو هذا الجسم في جهة أو كلياً كما في مثال المتن.
قوله: (صادق) أي في الجملة وهو ما إذا شهد به العقل على ما في شرح حكمة الإشراق، ويشير إليه قول الشارح فإن العقل إلخ فما قيل: من أن القول بأن حكم الوهم في المحسوسات صادق مطلقا، وإن صرحوا به، غلط، فإنه قد يحكم بعداوة من لا عداوة له ليس بشيء.
(The seventh: imaginal judgments in sensory matters.) For the judgment of the imagination (al-wahm) concerning sensory matters is true, such as 'every body is in a direction', for reason (al-'aql) affirms its judgments concerning sensory matters, and due to their agreement, the sciences that proceed like geometry are [established]. He said: (Imaginal judgments) He did not define them because of what was mentioned in the intuitive judgments. He said: (For the judgment of the imagination, etc.) This is a justification for the implied judgment, i.e., the imaginal judgments in sensory matters are only counted as certain because the judgment of the imagination, etc., whether it is particular, such as 'this body is in a direction', or universal, as in the example of the text. He said: (True) i.e., in general, and it is when reason bears witness to it, as in the commentary on Hikmat al-Ishraq. The commentator's statement indicates that, for reason, etc. So what is said – that the statement that the judgment of the imagination concerning sensory matters is absolutely true, even if they explicitly state it, is a mistake, for it may judge enmity regarding one who has no enmity – is nothing.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh type of certain premises: imaginal judgments (al-wahmiyyat) as applied to sensory things. The author states that the imagination's judgment in sensory matters is true, giving the example 'every body is in a direction', and explains that reason confirms these judgments because they match sensory experience, resulting in sciences like geometry. The commentary then clarifies that the term 'imaginal judgments' is not defined again due to prior discussion in the section on intuitive judgments. The justification is that these judgments are considered certain whether particular or universal. The passage further qualifies 'true' as meaning 'in general', not absolutely, because the imagination can err in particular cases like imagining enmity where none exists. Thus, it guards against assuming unconditional truth of all imaginal judgments.

Correction: The passage clarifies that these judgments are true only in general (في الجملة). Reason must confirm them, and they can be false in specific cases, e.g., mistakenly imagining enmity.
