The Objection of the Nine Probabilities and Its Answer
Objection and answer regarding the nine probabilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection about the nine probabilities is answered by distinguishing between textual and mental contextual indicators.

Core Idea
Textual negation does not exclude mental contextual indicators.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a crucial objection: if a contextual indicator negates all nine probabilities, including figurative language, then the literal meaning is certain. How can we avoid contradictions? The answer lies in distinguishing probabilities that arise from the wording itself from those that may arise from mental considerations. Let's examine the exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
2
Identify the objection
The objector assumes the contextual indicator negates all nine probabilities, including majaz. If majaz is negated, literal meaning is certain, else the speaker would be lying. Find this logic in the text.
3
Identify the answer
The author clarifies that the negation only pertains to probabilities stemming from the wording itself. It does not rule out a mental contextual indicator (like impossibility) that could indicate non-intention.
Notice the key phrase: 'بالنظر إلى نفس الألفاظ'
4
Understand the role of mental contextual indicator
A mental contextual indicator, such as impossibility, can override the apparent literal meaning. This prevents the need for the speaker to be lying.
5
Reflect on significance
This distinction is vital for interpreting divine speech: textual cues do not exhaust meaning; rational considerations also play a role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المفروض أن القرينة دالة على انتفاء الاحتمالات التسعة ومن جملتها المجاز فإذا انتفى المجاز تعين كون معناه الحقيقي مراداً للمتكلم، فيحصل الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي وإلا لزم كذب القائل الصادق قلت : قد عرفت أن المراد أنها تدل على انتفاء تلك الأمور بالنظر إلى نفس الألفاظ بأن ليس في اللفظ ما يدل على واحد من تلك الأمور، وهو لا يقتضي انتفاء التجوز مطلقاً لجواز وجود القرينة العقلية على عدم الإرادة كالامتناع فيما نحن فيه .
For it is said: The assumption is that the contextual indicator indicates the negation of the nine probabilities, among which is figurative language (majaz). If figurative language is negated, then its literal meaning is necessarily intended by the speaker, so certainty of the absence of a mental contrary is obtained; otherwise, the truthful speaker would be lying. I say: You already know that what is meant is that it indicates the negation of those matters with respect to the words themselves, in that there is nothing in the word that indicates any one of those matters. And that does not necessitate the negation of figurative language absolutely, because of the possibility of the existence of a mental contextual indicator indicating non-intention, such as impossibility in what we are discussing.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if the contextual indicator (qarina) negates all nine probabilities (including figurative language), then the literal meaning is certain, and thus no mental contrary remains—otherwise the truthful speaker would be lying. The answer clarifies that the contextual indicator only negates probabilities that are based on the wording itself; it does not rule out a mental contextual indicator (like the impossibility of the literal meaning) that could indicate the speaker did not intend that meaning. Such a mental indicator can override the assumed literal interpretation, especially when the subject is impossible.

Key Terms
الاحتمالات التسعة
المجاز
القرينة العقلية
الإرادة
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating textual probabilities always leads to certainty of literal meaning.
Correction: The author shows that only probabilities arising from wording are negated; mental contextual indicators can still override.

Checkpoint
What does the answer say about the negation of the nine probabilities?
A) It absolutely negates figurative language
B) It only negates probabilities based on wording, not mental indicators
C) It proves the speaker intended the literal meaning beyond doubt
D) It eliminates the possibility of impossibility

Key Takeaway
When interpreting scripture, the absence of textual indicators for figurative meaning does not rule out mental indicators like impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an that seems to say something impossible or contradictory to reason, do you automatically assume you must accept the impossibility or do you pause to consider a deeper meaning? How does this lesson challenge you to take mental indicators seriously in your own faith practice?
?
In your daily life, you often interpret what others say by looking beyond their words to context and reason. Are you as careful when interpreting Allah's words? What would change if you applied the same rational scrutiny to scripture that you apply to human speech?
?
This lesson warns against assuming certainty based only on the absence of textual indicators. Where in your own understanding of Islam do you think you might have held false certainty because you ignored mental contextual indicators? Will you revisit that certainty today?

Objection: Can Transmission Yield Certainty in Intellectual Matters?
Sanusi responds to the claim that some intellectual matters whose possibility is known by intellect can be known with certainty through transmitted proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmission does not yield certainty in intellectual matters even when their possibility is known by intellect, and how the response distinguishes between religious and intellectual matters.

Core Idea
Transmission yields certainty only where reason has no independent access.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection: if an intellectual matter's possibility is proven by intellect, why can't transmitted proofs about it yield certainty? The response draws a sharp distinction between religious matters—where reason has no independent access—and intellectual matters, where a contrary rational proof may always emerge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objector's claim: if possibility is known by intellect, transmission should give certainty. What is the apparent inconsistency?
The objector is trying to show that the previous argument leads to a contradiction.
2
Grasp the first part of the response
Sanusi says that in all religious matters (shar`iyat), transmitted proofs with contextual indicators yield certainty, unlike intellectual matters. Notice the key difference: reason has no path in religious matters.
The phrase 'la tariqa lil-aql' indicates reason's inability to independently judge religious matters.
3
Understand the second part
For intellectual matters, even if possibility is known, one might later find an intellectual proof contrary to the transmission, requiring metaphorical interpretation. This is why transmission does not give certainty.
This only applies if the matter's possibility is not already established by a certain intellectual proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وحينئذ جاز أن يكون من (الممتنعات) فإن قلت مقتضى هذا الكلام أن بعض العقليات التي يثبت إمكانها بالقاطع العقلي يفيد النقل فيها القطع، فما الفرق فيها حينئذ بينهما ؟ قلت كل الشرعيات يفيد الدليل النقلي المقارن للقرائن القطع فيه بخلاف كل العقليات، وأيضاً لا طريق للعقل في النقليات بخلاف العقليات الممكنة قطعاً، إذ ربما نجد دليلاً عقلياً على خلاف ما ورد به النقل، فتأول لكن هذا إنما يظهر إذا لم يثبت إمكانها بالدليل العقلي اليقيني
His statement: '(And in that case it is possible that it is among the impossible things)' Then if you say: The implication of this speech is that some intellectual matters whose possibility is established by a decisive intellectual proof, transmission (al-naql) yields certainty in them, so what then is the difference between them? I say: All religious matters (shar`iyat) - the transmitted proof accompanied by contextual indicators yields certainty in them, unlike all intellectual matters. Also, reason has no path in transmitted matters (naqliyat), unlike intellectual matters that are certainly possible, for perhaps we find an intellectual proof contrary to what the transmission brought, so it is interpreted metaphorically. But this only appears if its possibility is not established by a certain intellectual proof.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion with a new objection. The objector points out that if the transmission deals with an intellectual matter whose possibility has been proven by a certain intellectual proof, then the transmitted proof should yield certainty in that matter, just like in religious matters. The answer distinguishes: in all religious matters, the transmitted proof with its contextual indicators yields certainty, but not in all intellectual matters. Moreover, reason has no independent access to religious matters, so there is no risk of a conflicting intellectual proof. In intellectual matters, even if their possibility is known, one might later find an intellectual proof that contradicts the transmission, requiring metaphorical interpretation. However, this only applies if the matter's possibility is not already established by a certain intellectual proof; if it is established, the transmission can still be vulnerable to a contrary intellectual proof through interpretation.

Key Terms
القاطع العقلي
النقليات
الفرق
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a matter's possibility is known by intellect, transmission gives certainty in that matter.
Correction: The author shows that the possibility of a contrary intellectual proof remains, so transmission cannot yield certainty; it may require metaphorical interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why do transmitted proofs in intellectual matters not yield certainty even when possibility is known by intellect?
A) Because reason has no path in religious matters
B) Because one might later find an intellectual proof to the contrary
C) Because all intellectual matters are impossible
D) Because transmitted proofs never yield certainty

Key Takeaway
Transmission yields certainty only where reason has no independent access; in intellectual matters, the possibility of a conflicting rational proof prevents certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a scholar say something about a matter of belief, do you automatically assume it is certain because it comes from a reliable source, or do you first ask whether reason has any independent access to that matter? How will you change this habit after studying Sanusi's distinction?
?
Think of a time when you had to reinterpret your understanding of a religious text because it seemed to conflict with a rational conclusion. Did that feel like a weakness or a strength in your faith? How does this lesson's teaching about ta'wil reshape your view of such moments?
?
Are you sometimes afraid to use your intellect in religious matters, worried it might lead you away from certainty? Sanusi shows that transmission yields certainty only where reason has no path, and reason itself is a gift. What is one step you can take this week to engage your intellect more deeply in a matter where reason does have a path, while remaining open to ta'wil?

The Famous Research on Transmitted Proofs
Sanusi presents the famous research and argues for certainty from transmitted proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of the famous research: that transmitted proofs can yield certainty in intellectual matters because the truthfulness of the speaker applies universally and possibilities extend beyond what reason rules out.

Core Idea
The truthfulness of the speaker and the possibility of the unjudged extend certainty to intellectual matters.

This lesson explores a famous research that challenges the earlier limitation of transmitted proofs to religious matters. The argument hinges on the truthfulness of the speaker and the scope of what the intellect can declare impossible. We will see how the author defends the application of transmitted proofs to intellectual matters.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
Recognize that the famous research asserts that transmitted proofs yield certainty in intellectual matters, countering the earlier position.
This is a direct response to the previous lesson's limitation.
2
Examine the basis
The basis is twofold: (1) the truthfulness of the speaker applies to all matters, (2) what the intellect does not judge impossible should be considered possible, so transmitted statements in intellectual matters are possible.
This prevents contradiction between transmitted and rational proofs.
3
Review the commentator's interpretation
The author presents a possible interpretation of the commentator: that transmitted proofs yield certainty because they are composed of intuitive premises that cannot be contradicted.
This interpretation is necessary to salvage certainty from the commentator's earlier view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي ها هنا بحث مشهور وهو أن المبني لعدم المعارض العقلي في الشرعيات صدق القائل، وهو قائم في العقليات أيضاً وما لا يحكم العقل بإمكانه ثبوتاً، وانتفاء لا يلزم أن يكون من الممتنعات لجواز إمكانه الخالي من العقل، فينبغي أن يحمل كل ما علم أن الشرع نطق به على هذا القسم، لئلا يلزم كذبه وإبطال قطع العقل بصدقه فالحق أن النقلي أيضاً يفيد القطع في العقليات أيضاً، ولا يفيد ما ذكره الشارح ولا مخلص إلا بأن يقال : مراده أن النظر في الأدلة أنفسها والقرائن في الشرعيات يفيد الجزم بعدم المعارض لأجل إفادته الإرادة من القائل تفيد الجزم بعدم المعارض لأنها مركبة من مقدمات علم بالبديهة صحتها أو علم بالبديهة لزومها مما علم صحته بالبديهة، وحينئذ يستحيل أن يوجد ما يعارضها لأن أحكام البديهة لا تتعارض بحسب نفس الأمر أصلاً كما مر، وقد جزم الإمام الرازي بأنه لا يجوز التمسك بالأدلة النقلية في المسائل العقلية نعم يجوز التمسك بها في المسائل النقلية تارة لإفادة اليقين كما في مسألة حجية الإجماع، وخبر الآحاد وأخرى لإفادة الظن كما في الأحكام الشرعية الفرعية . قوله : (لا تتعارض في نفس الأمر وإلا لزم تحقق النقيضين في نفس الأمر، وإنما قيد بذلك لأنها قد تتعارض عند العقل بناء على اشتباه الحكم البديهي بالوهمي . قوله : ( وقد جزم إلخ) وذلك لأن احتمال أن يكون للقرينة مدخل في الجزم المذكور كاف في عدم إفادة اليقين في العقليات ولا يتوقف على الجزم به ثبوتاً تم ما يتعلق بالموقف الأول بعون الله وحسن توفيقه. الصادق جزماً وفي العقليات إفادته الجزم بعدمه محل نظر بناء على أن إفادته الإرادة محل له لا أنه بعد ما علم مراد الشارع يقيناً في العقلي والنقلي يحصل الجزم بعدم المعارض في الثاني دون الأول فإنه غير مسلم .
There remains here a famous research (bahth mashhur): The foundation for the absence of a mental contrary in religious matters is the truthfulness of the speaker, and that also applies to intellectual matters. What the intellect does not judge as possible or impossible does not necessarily have to be among the impossible things, because of the possibility of its possibility (imkan) that is beyond reason. Therefore, everything that is known that the Law (shar`) has spoken about should be interpreted in this category, lest lying be necessitated and the intellect's certainty of his truthfulness be invalidated. So the truth is that the transmitted [proof] also yields certainty in intellectual matters. And what the commentator (al-sharih) mentioned does not benefit, and there is no escape except to say: His meaning is that consideration of the proofs themselves and the contextual indicators in religious matters yields certainty of the absence of a contrary because they indicate the intention from the speaker. [They] yield certainty of the absence of a contrary because they are composed of premises whose correctness is known by intuition (badiha) or whose implication is known by intuition from what is known to be correct by intuition. In that case, it is impossible for anything to oppose them because the judgments of intuition do not contradict each other in reality at all, as previously mentioned. And Imam al-Razi has decisively held that it is not permissible to rely on transmitted proofs in intellectual matters; yes, it is permissible to rely on them in transmitted matters, sometimes to yield certainty as in the case of the authority of consensus (ijma`) and the report of individuals (khabar al-ahad), and other times to yield probability (zann) as in subsidiary legal rulings. His statement: 'They do not contradict in reality, otherwise the existence of two opposites in reality would be necessitated.' He only qualified it with that because they might contradict in the mind based on confusion of an intuitive judgment with an imaginary one. His statement: 'And he has decisively held, etc.' That is because the possibility that a contextual indicator has an entry in the mentioned certainty is sufficient for not yielding certainty in intellectual matters, and it does not depend on certainty of its existence. End of what relates to the First Stand (al-mawqif al-awwal) with God's help and good success. [As for] the truthful speaker with certainty, and in intellectual matters its yielding certainty of its absence is a matter of consideration, because that its indication of intention is a matter for it, not that after knowing the intention of the Lawgiver with certainty in both intellectual and transmitted [matters], certainty of the absence of a contrary is obtained in the second without the first, for that is not granted.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the famous research (bahth mashhur) that challenges the earlier distinction. It argues that the basis for lacking a mental contrary in religious matters is the truthfulness of the speaker, which also applies to intellectual matters. Moreover, what the intellect does not definitely deem impossible should be considered possible, so transmitted proofs in intellectual matters should yield certainty. The author then states that the truth is that transmitted proofs do yield certainty in intellectual matters as well. The commentator's view is insufficient unless interpreted as saying that the transmitted proofs in religious matters yield certainty because they are composed of intuitive premises that cannot be contradicted. Then Imam al-Razi's position is mentioned: he prohibits using transmitted proofs in intellectual matters, but allows them in transmitted matters for certainty (like consensus and mutawatir) or probability (like isolated reports). The passage ends with a note that intuitive judgments do not contradict each other in reality, and the final remark that even if the speaker is known to be truthful, the lack of certainty in intellectual matters is still open to consideration because the indication of intention is itself a matter of analysis, not a given.

Key Terms
البحث المشهور
صدق القائل
البديهة
أحكام البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The truthfulness of the speaker alone suffices for certainty in all matters without further analysis.
Correction: The research shows that the truthfulness of the speaker, combined with the possibility of what is not ruled out by reason, allows certainty, but the commentator's interpretation adds that intuitive premises are necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the famous research, why can transmitted proofs yield certainty in intellectual matters?
A) Because the truthfulness of the speaker applies to all matters and what is not known to be impossible can be considered possible.
B) Because intuitive judgments never contradict each other.
C) Because Imam al-Razi permitted it.
D) Because the commentator's view is sufficient as it stands.

Key Takeaway
The famous research shows that the truthfulness of the speaker and the possibility of the unjudged extend certainty from transmitted proofs to intellectual matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a revealed truth about an issue like the afterlife that your intellect struggles to grasp, do you dismiss it as impossible or consider it possible beyond your reason? This lesson forces you to examine: are you truly trusting the Truthfulness of the Speaker, or are you secretly prioritizing your own limited judgment? Name one specific belief from revelation that you have resisted because it seemed intellectually improbable, and commit to re-examining it this week with the principle of possibility in mind.
?
You rely on your intuition (badiha) for many things—but have you ever considered that your intuitive judgments might contradict a revealed truth? This lesson says intuitive judgments cannot contradict each other in reality. So if you feel a contradiction, where is the error: in your understanding of revelation or in your supposed intuition? Write down one area where you feel tension between what you think is 'obvious' and what Allah has said, and then ask yourself: which one is truly intuitive and which one is merely cultural or personal bias?
?
The transmitted proof gives certainty—but only if it is built on premises you know by intuition. This means you must actively verify that your foundations are sound. Take a moment: what are the foundational beliefs about God, prophethood, and the unseen that you hold? Are they based on intuitive knowledge or on blind imitation? If your certainty has never been tested by a challenge like this research, how can you be sure it is genuine? Design a simple personal test this week to verify one of your foundational beliefs through the lens of why it is certain.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Imam al-Razi's Restriction of Transmitted Proofs
Sanusi reports al-Razi's view and adds a concluding remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's position that transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty in intellectual matters but only in transmitted matters, and the reasons behind this restriction.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs are confined to transmitted matters; intellectual matters require purely rational proofs.

This lesson examines the strong opposition to the famous research by Imam al-Razi, who denied the use of transmitted proofs in intellectual matters. We will also see the exceptions he allowed in transmitted matters and the final note about the non-contradiction of intuitive judgments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize al-Razi's decisive rejection
Imam al-Razi decisively holds that transmitted proofs cannot be used in intellectual matters.
This is a direct counter to the famous research.
2
Understand the permitted uses
In transmitted matters, al-Razi allows transmitted proofs to yield certainty for consensus and the mutawatir report, and probability for subsidiary legal rulings.
The certainty here is not about intellectual content but about religious authority.
3
Reflect on the final remark
The passage ends with a note that even if the truthfulness of the speaker is certain, the indication of intention is subject to analysis, so certainty in intellectual matters is not automatic.
This nuance qualifies the entire debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي ها هنا بحث مشهور وهو أن المبني لعدم المعارض العقلي في الشرعيات صدق القائل، وهو قائم في العقليات أيضاً وما لا يحكم العقل بإمكانه ثبوتاً، وانتفاء لا يلزم أن يكون من الممتنعات لجواز إمكانه الخالي من العقل، فينبغي أن يحمل كل ما علم أن الشرع نطق به على هذا القسم، لئلا يلزم كذبه وإبطال قطع العقل بصدقه فالحق أن النقلي أيضاً يفيد القطع في العقليات أيضاً، ولا يفيد ما ذكره الشارح ولا مخلص إلا بأن يقال : مراده أن النظر في الأدلة أنفسها والقرائن في الشرعيات يفيد الجزم بعدم المعارض لأجل إفادته الإرادة من القائل تفيد الجزم بعدم المعارض لأنها مركبة من مقدمات علم بالبديهة صحتها أو علم بالبديهة لزومها مما علم صحته بالبديهة، وحينئذ يستحيل أن يوجد ما يعارضها لأن أحكام البديهة لا تتعارض بحسب نفس الأمر أصلاً كما مر، وقد جزم الإمام الرازي بأنه لا يجوز التمسك بالأدلة النقلية في المسائل العقلية نعم يجوز التمسك بها في المسائل النقلية تارة لإفادة اليقين كما في مسألة حجية الإجماع، وخبر الآحاد وأخرى لإفادة الظن كما في الأحكام الشرعية الفرعية . قوله : (لا تتعارض في نفس الأمر وإلا لزم تحقق النقيضين في نفس الأمر، وإنما قيد بذلك لأنها قد تتعارض عند العقل بناء على اشتباه الحكم البديهي بالوهمي . قوله : ( وقد جزم إلخ) وذلك لأن احتمال أن يكون للقرينة مدخل في الجزم المذكور كاف في عدم إفادة اليقين في العقليات ولا يتوقف على الجزم به ثبوتاً تم ما يتعلق بالموقف الأول بعون الله وحسن توفيقه. الصادق جزماً وفي العقليات إفادته الجزم بعدمه محل نظر بناء على أن إفادته الإرادة محل له لا أنه بعد ما علم مراد الشارع يقيناً في العقلي والنقلي يحصل الجزم بعدم المعارض في الثاني دون الأول فإنه غير مسلم .
There remains here a famous research (bahth mashhur): The foundation for the absence of a mental contrary in religious matters is the truthfulness of the speaker, and that also applies to intellectual matters. What the intellect does not judge as possible or impossible does not necessarily have to be among the impossible things, because of the possibility of its possibility (imkan) that is beyond reason. Therefore, everything that is known that the Law (shar`) has spoken about should be interpreted in this category, lest lying be necessitated and the intellect's certainty of his truthfulness be invalidated. So the truth is that the transmitted [proof] also yields certainty in intellectual matters. And what the commentator (al-sharih) mentioned does not benefit, and there is no escape except to say: His meaning is that consideration of the proofs themselves and the contextual indicators in religious matters yields certainty of the absence of a contrary because they indicate the intention from the speaker. [They] yield certainty of the absence of a contrary because they are composed of premises whose correctness is known by intuition (badiha) or whose implication is known by intuition from what is known to be correct by intuition. In that case, it is impossible for anything to oppose them because the judgments of intuition do not contradict each other in reality at all, as previously mentioned. And Imam al-Razi has decisively held that it is not permissible to rely on transmitted proofs in intellectual matters; yes, it is permissible to rely on them in transmitted matters, sometimes to yield certainty as in the case of the authority of consensus (ijma`) and the report of individuals (khabar al-ahad), and other times to yield probability (zann) as in subsidiary legal rulings. His statement: 'They do not contradict in reality, otherwise the existence of two opposites in reality would be necessitated.' He only qualified it with that because they might contradict in the mind based on confusion of an intuitive judgment with an imaginary one. His statement: 'And he has decisively held, etc.' That is because the possibility that a contextual indicator has an entry in the mentioned certainty is sufficient for not yielding certainty in intellectual matters, and it does not depend on certainty of its existence. End of what relates to the First Stand (al-mawqif al-awwal) with God's help and good success. [As for] the truthful speaker with certainty, and in intellectual matters its yielding certainty of its absence is a matter of consideration, because that its indication of intention is a matter for it, not that after knowing the intention of the Lawgiver with certainty in both intellectual and transmitted [matters], certainty of the absence of a contrary is obtained in the second without the first, for that is not granted.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the famous research (bahth mashhur) that challenges the earlier distinction. It argues that the basis for lacking a mental contrary in religious matters is the truthfulness of the speaker, which also applies to intellectual matters. Moreover, what the intellect does not definitely deem impossible should be considered possible, so transmitted proofs in intellectual matters should yield certainty. The author then states that the truth is that transmitted proofs do yield certainty in intellectual matters as well. The commentator's view is insufficient unless interpreted as saying that the transmitted proofs in religious matters yield certainty because they are composed of intuitive premises that cannot be contradicted. Then Imam al-Razi's position is mentioned: he prohibits using transmitted proofs in intellectual matters, but allows them in transmitted matters for certainty (like consensus and mutawatir) or probability (like isolated reports). The passage ends with a note that intuitive judgments do not contradict each other in reality, and the final remark that even if the speaker is known to be truthful, the lack of certainty in intellectual matters is still open to consideration because the indication of intention is itself a matter of analysis, not a given.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam al-Razi completely rejected all transmitted proofs.
Correction: Al-Razi rejected their use only in intellectual matters; he allowed them in transmitted matters for certainty (consensus, mutawatir) and probability (ahad rulings).

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, in which matters can transmitted proofs yield certainty?
A) In intellectual matters only.
B) In transmitted matters only, specifically for consensus and the mutawatir report.
C) In all matters, both intellectual and transmitted.
D) In no matters at all.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi restricts transmitted proofs to transmitted matters, with certainty for consensus and mutawatir, and probability for ahad reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a core belief about Allah, the Prophet, or the unseen solely because you heard it in a lecture or read it in a book, without rationally verifying it? Will you now commit to examining each claim with the intellectual rigor al-Razi demands?
?
When you hear a hadith or an opinion from a scholar, do you automatically treat it as absolute truth, or do you assess whether it yields certainty or probability? Are you prepared to admit that some of what you hold as yaqīn may only be ẓann?
?
Al-Razi’s argument challenges the use of transmitted proofs in matters of aqeedah. Yet many Muslims rely entirely on transmitted evidence for their beliefs. Could your own faith be built on a foundation that al-Razi would deem insufficient? What concrete steps will you take to ensure your beliefs are grounded in rational certainty?
